Judge’s Procedure When Slave’s Description Mismatches the Letter
Procedural ruling for judges on verifying slave descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios for a judge receiving a slave-related letter and explain the procedural ruling in each scenario.

Core Idea
Verify description, reject mismatch; on match, hand over with guarantor, not final judgment.

When a judge receives a letter about a disputed slave, he must first verify the slave's physical description against the witnesses' testimony. The source outlines two clear possibilities: a mismatch leading to rejection of the letter, or a match requiring a careful handover with a guarantor and seal. This lesson unpacks the judge's duties to ensure fairness and prevent unlawful seizure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Recognize that the judge must first check if the slave's description (hilya) matches what the witnesses testified. The text gives a binary outcome: mismatch or match.
2
Analyze the Mismatch Scenario
If the description contradicts the witnesses' testimony, the judge rejects the letter entirely. This prevents using an incorrectly identified slave.
The letter is nullified; no further action.
3
Analyze the Match Scenario
If the description matches, the judge checks whether the opponent (defendant) has already gone to Bukhara (the destination court). If so, the matter is concluded. Otherwise, the judge hands the slave to the plaintiff without final judgment, takes a guarantor, and places a lead seal around the slave’s neck.
Handing over is temporary; ownership is not decided.
4
Explain the Purpose of Guarantor and Seal
The guarantor (kafil) ensures the slave's availability for future proceedings. The lead seal prevents anyone from accusing the slave of being stolen during transit.
Both measures protect the slave and the integrity of the case.
5
Contrast Temporary Handover with Final Judgment
The judge does not adjudicate the slave to the plaintiff (iya qada), only entrusts him temporarily. This distinction is crucial: the plaintiff assumes custody but not ownership.
Confusing custody with final judgment is a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فإن لم يكن... الخ، يعني أن القاضي إذا نسخ الكتاب فلا يخلو : 1. إما أن يجد حلية العبدِ المذكور مخالفة لما شهد به الشهود عند القاضي الكاتب، فإذا ظهر أنَّ العبد المشهود به في الكتاب ردَّ هذا الكتاب. . أو يجد حليته موافقة لما في هذا الكتاب، فالخصم إن ذهب إلى البخارا فيها، وإلا دفع العبد إلى المدعي من غير أن يقضي له بالعبد، ويأخذ كفيلاً بنفس العبد، وجعل في عنق العبد خاتماً من رصاص، حتى لا يتعرض له أحد في الطريق أنه سرق.
He said: If it is not... etc., meaning the judge when copying the letter is either: 1. That he finds the description of the mentioned slave contradictory to what the witnesses testified before the writing judge, so when it appears that the slave testified about in the letter is different, he rejects this letter. 2. Or he finds his description matching what is in this letter, then if the opponent went to Bukhara, that is it, otherwise he hands the slave to the plaintiff without adjudicating the slave to him, and takes a guarantor for the slave himself, and puts a lead seal around the slave's neck, so that no one interferes with him on the road claiming he is stolen.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the procedure when a judge receives a letter regarding a disputed slave. Two scenarios: First, if the physical description (hilya) of the slave as recorded in the letter contradicts the testimony of witnesses before the writing judge, the receiving judge rejects the letter. Second, if the description matches, then if the opponent (defendant) has already gone to Bukhara (the destination court), the matter is concluded there. Otherwise, the receiving judge hands the slave to the plaintiff without final judgment (i.e., not as a court order) but takes a guarantor for the slave's return, and places a lead seal on the slave's neck to prevent anyone from accusing him of theft. This is a practical measure to secure the slave until the case is resolved. The ruling is procedural, not a substantive judgment on ownership.

Key Terms
الحلية (al-hilya): Physical description of a person, specifically the slave's distinctive marks.
الكفيل (al-kafil): A guarantor who ensures the slave's availability for future court proceedings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He releases the slave
B) He hands him to the plaintiff without judgment and takes a guarantor
C) He sends the slave back
D) He imprisons the slave

Key Takeaway
A judge must verify the slave's description; only on a match and with required safeguards (guarantor, seal) may the slave be temporarily handed to the plaintiff without final judgment.

Procedure for Transferring a Disputed Slave via Judge's Letter
Protocol for judge-to-judge case transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the step-by-step procedure for transferring a disputed slave from one judge to another via written letter, and identify the conditions for accepting the letter.

Core Idea
A judge's letter transmits testimony, not a final judgment; final judgment requires the opponent's presence.

When a judge receives a letter from another judge regarding a disputed slave, a careful procedure must be followed. The judge must verify the description, secure the slave with a guarantor and seal, and coordinate with the original judge. This lesson unpacks the protocol as detailed in the Hanafi juristic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Verify the slave's description
First, the receiving judge checks if the slave's physical description (hilya) matches what was written. If it does not match, he rejects the letter.
2
Handle the opponent's presence
If the opponent (defendant) is willing to go to the original judge's city, he goes. Otherwise, the judge hands the slave to the plaintiff but not as a final judgment.
This provisional handover requires a guarantor (kafil).
3
Secure the slave and write reply
The judge takes a guarantor from the plaintiff for the slave, seals the slave's neck to prevent substitution, and writes back to the original judge that the slave has been sent.
The seal and guarantee protect against fraud.
4
Original judge summons witnesses
The original judge summons the witnesses who testified in the slave's absence to testify again in the slave's presence. They confirm ownership, but the judge still does not issue a final judgment because the opponent is absent.
Final judgment requires the opponent's presence.
5
Conditions for accepting the letter
The judge must verify that the letter was read and sealed in the presence of the witnesses. The presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence on the letter, but not for accepting the letter itself.
This distinction is from Muhammad al-Shaybani.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : لا في حد وقود ؛ أي لا يقبل فيهما لما مرَّ من أنَّ مبناهما على الإسقاط بأدنى شبهة، وفي قبوله سعي في إثباتهما.
If his description is not as written, he abandons it. But if it is, then the opponent: if he goes with the slave to Bukhara, fine; otherwise, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff, not by way of judgment, and takes from him a guarantor for the slave himself, and puts something around his neck, and seals it as protection against substitution when the witnesses testify, and writes to the judge of Bukhara a reply to his letter, that he has sent the slave to him. When the letter reaches him, he summons the witnesses who testified in the absence of the slave to testify in his presence, and they point out that he is the property of the plaintiff, but he does not judge because the opponent is absent. Then he writes to the judge of Samarkand that the witnesses testified in his presence, so that the judge of Samarkand may judge against the opponent, and the guarantor is released from his guarantee.
The judge so-and-so, then the judge says to him: Bring evidence that this is the letter of that judge. So if the judge accepts it without the presence of his opponent, it is permissible.
And if he hears the evidence that this is the judge's letter without the presence of the opponent, it is not permissible. So the presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence on the letter, not a condition for accepting the letter. And it is the saying of Muhammad in al-Shurut: 'And if he accepts that and there is no opponent with him, it is permissible.' He intended by it acceptance of the letter, not acceptance of the evidence. So when the witnesses testify that it is the letter of Judge so-and-so son of so-and-so to you, and it is sealed with his seal, then he accepts the letter and says: Did he read it to you? And did he seal it in your presence? If they say no, or He read to us but did not seal in our presence, or vice versa, he does not take the letter. But if they say yes, he read to us, sealed in our presence, and made us witnesses, he opens the letter. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.
11] His saying: 'If it is not...' etc., means that the judge, when copying the letter, is not free from: 1. Either he finds the description of the mentioned slave contradictory to what the witnesses testified before the writing judge, so if it appears that the slave testified about in the letter, he rejects this letter. 2. Or he finds his description matching what is in this letter. Then the opponent: if he goes to Bukhara, fine; otherwise, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff without judging for him the slave, and takes a guarantor for the slave himself, and puts a lead seal around the slave's neck so that no one may accost him on the road claiming he stole.
(1) (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, 3:387)
[PAGE_452]
452
Zubdat al-Nihaya li 'Umdat al-Ri'aya
And from Muhammad: its acceptance in what is transportable, and later scholars rely on it, not in hadd and qisas.
[1]
(And from Muhammad: its acceptance in what is transportable, and later scholars rely on it, not in hadd and qisas.
And he writes a letter to the judge of Bukhara with that, and makes two witnesses on his letter and seal, and on what is in the letter. When the letter reaches the judge of Bukhara, and the witnesses testify that this is the letter of the judge of Samarkand and his seal, he orders the plaintiff to bring the witnesses who testified before him the first time, so that they may testify in the presence of the slave that he is the property of this plaintiff. When they testify thus, what does the judge of Bukhara do?
The narrations from Abu Yusuf differ: it is mentioned in some narrations that the judge of Bukhara does not judge for the plaintiff the slave, but writes another letter to the judge of Samarkand, and writes in it what transpired before him, and makes two witnesses on his letter and seal and what is in it, and sends the slave with him to Samarkand. And the two witnesses testify before him about the letter and seal and what is in the letter, and the justice of the witnesses becomes evident, he judges for the plaintiff the slave in the presence of the defendant, and releases the guarantor of the plaintiff. And in another narration: that the judge of Bukhara judges the slave for the plaintiff, and writes to the judge of Samarkand so that the guarantor of the plaintiff is released. And according to the narration in which Abu Yusuf permitted the judge's letter regarding female slaves, its form: what we mentioned regarding the male slave, except that if the plaintiff is not trustworthy and reliable, the judge to whom the letter is written does not hand her over to him, but orders the plaintiff to bring a trustworthy and reliable man in his religion and intellect to send her with him; because precaution in the matter of female private parts is obligatory. Thus in al-Muhit and other reliable works.
[1] His saying: 'And later scholars rely on it'; and on it is the fatwa. Thus in al-Minhah, and in al-Bazzaziyya: The earlier scholars did not take the saying of the second imam, and the practice of the jurists today is on permissibility in everything due to need. End. And al-Ijabi said to him: And on it is the fatwa.
[2] His saying: 'Not in hadd and qisas'; i.e., it is not accepted in these two, because of what has passed that their foundation is on dropping by the slightest doubt, and in accepting it is an effort to establish them.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the procedure for a judge sending a disputed slave to another judge via a written letter (kitab al-qadi). It is from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. The passage begins by explaining what the judge does when he receives a letter from another judge regarding a slave. First, he must verify that the description (hilya) of the slave matches what was testified. If it does not match, he rejects the letter. If it matches, he must check whether the opponent (the defendant) is willing to go to the original judge's city. If not, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff, but not as a final judgment; he takes a guarantor (kafil) from the plaintiff for the slave's safe return, and seals the slave's neck to prevent substitution. He then writes back to the first judge. When the first judge receives this reply, he summons the witnesses who testified in the slave's absence to testify again in his presence, pointing out that the slave belongs to the plaintiff. However, he still does not issue a final judgment because the defendant is absent. Instead, he writes to the judge of Samarkand (the original judge) that the witnesses have testified in the presence of the slave, so that the Samarkand judge can issue a final judgment against the defendant, thereby releasing the guarantor.
The passage also discusses the conditions for accepting the letter itself. The judge must ask the witnesses if the letter was read and sealed in their presence. If not, he does not accept it. The presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence on the letter, but not for accepting the letter itself. This is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Then the text brings an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Muhammad, the acceptance of the judge's letter is valid in cases involving transferable property (ma yunqal), but not in cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) have relied on this view, and it is the mufta bihi position. Earlier scholars had rejected Muhammad's view, but due to necessity, contemporary practice allows it in all cases. The passage also mentions a difference in narration from Abu Yusuf: in one narration, the second judge does not give the slave to the plaintiff but sends him back; in another, he does. Additionally, for female slaves (imaa'), Abu Yusuf permitted the judge's letter, but with extra precaution: the plaintiff must be trustworthy; otherwise, the slave is sent with a reliable third party.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitāb al-qāḍī): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a legal case, typically detailing testimony and requesting action.
كفيل (kafīl): A guarantor who ensures the return of the disputed property (here, the slave) if the case requires it.
حلية (hilya): Physical description of the slave, used for identification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the witnesses verify for the judge's letter to be accepted?
A) That the seal is intact
B) That the letter was read and sealed in their presence
C) That the opponent was present when the letter was written
D) That the plaintiff is trustworthy

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter transmits testimony, not a final judgment; verification of the seal and reading in witnesses' presence is essential for acceptance.

10 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Acceptance of Judge's Letter in Hudud and Qisas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of kitab al-qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting a judge's letter in hudud and qisas cases, explain the reasoning of both sides, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Hudud and qisas are based on dropping by doubt; accepting a judge's letter would be an effort to establish them, contradicting their foundation.

While the judge's letter is generally accepted for transferable property, its acceptance in criminal cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation) is disputed within the Hanafi school. Muhammad al-Shaybani restricts it, while earlier scholars allowed it. This lesson examines the evidence and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
The judge's letter (kitab al-qadi) is accepted in cases involving transferable property (ma yunqal). This is the basic ruling.
2
Identify the dispute
The question is whether the letter is also accepted in hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation).
3
Examine Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is not accepted in these categories. His reasoning: hudud and qisas are founded on dropping (isqat) by the slightest doubt, and accepting a letter would be an effort to establish them.
This is the view relied upon by later scholars (muta'akhkhirun).
4
Examine the opposing view
Earlier Hanafi scholars (mutaqaddimun) and apparently Abu Yusuf allowed acceptance in all cases, including hudud and qisas, based on general practice and the authority of the judge's communication.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later scholars' view (Muhammad's restriction) is the mufta bihi, but due to necessity (haja), contemporary practice permits acceptance in all cases.
Note the shift due to practical need.

If his description is not as written, he abandons it. But if it is, then the opponent: if he goes with the slave to Bukhara, fine; otherwise, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff, not by way of judgment, and takes from him a guarantor for the slave himself, and puts something around his neck, and seals it as protection against substitution when the witnesses testify, and writes to the judge of Bukhara a reply to his letter, that he has sent the slave to him. When the letter reaches him, he summons the witnesses who testified in the absence of the slave to testify in his presence, and they point out that he is the property of the plaintiff, but he does not judge because the opponent is absent. Then he writes to the judge of Samarkand that the witnesses testified in his presence, so that the judge of Samarkand may judge against the opponent, and the guarantor is released from his guarantee.
The judge so-and-so, then the judge says to him: Bring evidence that this is the letter of that judge. So if the judge accepts it without the presence of his opponent, it is permissible.
And if he hears the evidence that this is the judge's letter without the presence of the opponent, it is not permissible. So the presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence on the letter, not a condition for accepting the letter. And it is the saying of Muhammad in al-Shurut: 'And if he accepts that and there is no opponent with him, it is permissible.' He intended by it acceptance of the letter, not acceptance of the evidence. So when the witnesses testify that it is the letter of Judge so-and-so son of so-and-so to you, and it is sealed with his seal, then he accepts the letter and says: Did he read it to you? And did he seal it in your presence? If they say no, or He read to us but did not seal in our presence, or vice versa, he does not take the letter. But if they say yes, he read to us, sealed in our presence, and made us witnesses, he opens the letter. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.
11] His saying: 'If it is not...' etc., means that the judge, when copying the letter, is not free from: 1. Either he finds the description of the mentioned slave contradictory to what the witnesses testified before the writing judge, so if it appears that the slave testified about in the letter, he rejects this letter. 2. Or he finds his description matching what is in this letter. Then the opponent: if he goes to Bukhara, fine; otherwise, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff without judging for him the slave, and takes a guarantor for the slave himself, and puts a lead seal around the slave's neck so that no one may accost him on the road claiming he stole.

Zubdat al-Nihaya li 'Umdat al-Ri'aya
And from Muhammad: its acceptance in what is transportable, and later scholars rely on it, not in hadd and qisas.
[1]
(And from Muhammad: its acceptance in what is transportable, and later scholars rely on it, not in hadd and qisas.
And he writes a letter to the judge of Bukhara with that, and makes two witnesses on his letter and seal, and on what is in the letter. When the letter reaches the judge of Bukhara, and the witnesses testify that this is the letter of the judge of Samarkand and his seal, he orders the plaintiff to bring the witnesses who testified before him the first time, so that they may testify in the presence of the slave that he is the property of this plaintiff. When they testify thus, what does the judge of Bukhara do?
The narrations from Abu Yusuf differ: it is mentioned in some narrations that the judge of Bukhara does not judge for the plaintiff the slave, but writes another letter to the judge of Samarkand, and writes in it what transpired before him, and makes two witnesses on his letter and seal and what is in it, and sends the slave with him to Samarkand. And the two witnesses testify before him about the letter and seal and what is in the letter, and the justice of the witnesses becomes evident, he judges for the plaintiff the slave in the presence of the defendant, and releases the guarantor of the plaintiff. And in another narration: that the judge of Bukhara judges the slave for the plaintiff, and writes to the judge of Samarkand so that the guarantor of the plaintiff is released. And according to the narration in which Abu Yusuf permitted the judge's letter regarding female slaves, its form: what we mentioned regarding the male slave, except that if the plaintiff is not trustworthy and reliable, the judge to whom the letter is written does not hand her over to him, but orders the plaintiff to bring a trustworthy and reliable man in his religion and intellect to send her with him; because precaution in the matter of female private parts is obligatory. Thus in al-Muhit and other reliable works.
[1] His saying: 'And later scholars rely on it'; and on it is the fatwa. Thus in al-Minhah, and in al-Bazzaziyya: The earlier scholars did not take the saying of the second imam, and the practice of the jurists today is on permissibility in everything due to need. End. And al-Ijabi said to him: And on it is the fatwa.
[2] His saying: 'Not in hadd and qisas'; i.e., it is not accepted in these two, because of what has passed that their foundation is on dropping by the slightest doubt, and in accepting it is an effort to establish them.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the procedure for a judge sending a disputed slave to another judge via a written letter (kitab al-qadi). It is from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. The passage begins by explaining what the judge does when he receives a letter from another judge regarding a slave. First, he must verify that the description (hilya) of the slave matches what was testified. If it does not match, he rejects the letter. If it matches, he must check whether the opponent (the defendant) is willing to go to the original judge's city. If not, he hands over the slave to the plaintiff, but not as a final judgment; he takes a guarantor (kafil) from the plaintiff for the slave's safe return, and seals the slave's neck to prevent substitution. He then writes back to the first judge. When the first judge receives this reply, he summons the witnesses who testified in the slave's absence to testify again in his presence, pointing out that the slave belongs to the plaintiff. However, he still does not issue a final judgment because the defendant is absent. Instead, he writes to the judge of Samarkand (the original judge) that the witnesses have testified in the presence of the slave, so that the Samarkand judge can issue a final judgment against the defendant, thereby releasing the guarantor.
The passage also discusses the conditions for accepting the letter itself. The judge must ask the witnesses if the letter was read and sealed in their presence. If not, he does not accept it. The presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence on the letter, but not for accepting the letter itself. This is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Then the text brings an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Muhammad, the acceptance of the judge's letter is valid in cases involving transferable property (ma yunqal), but not in cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) have relied on this view, and it is the mufta bihi position. Earlier scholars had rejected Muhammad's view, but due to necessity, contemporary practice allows it in all cases. The passage also mentions a difference in narration from Abu Yusuf: in one narration, the second judge does not give the slave to the plaintiff but sends him back; in another, he does. Additionally, for female slaves (imaa'), Abu Yusuf permitted the judge's letter, but with extra precaution: the plaintiff must be trustworthy; otherwise, the slave is sent with a reliable third party.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Allah, such as for theft, zina, etc.
قود/قصاص (qawad/qisas): Retaliation punishment, typically for murder or injury.
مُتقدّمون (mutaqaddimūn): Earlier Hanafi scholars (e.g., Abu Hanifa's immediate students and early jurists).
مُتأخّرون (muta'akhkhirūn): Later Hanafi scholars (e.g., from the 6th century onwards).
إسقاط بأدنى شبهة (isqāt bi adnā shubha): The principle that hudud and qisas are to be dropped at the slightest doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in the text for not accepting the judge's letter in hudud and qisas?
A) The letter might be forged
B) Hudud and qisas are based on dropping by the slightest doubt
C) The witnesses must be present
D) The opponent is absent

Key Takeaway
Hudud and qisas are excluded from the judge's letter because their foundation is on dropping by doubt; accepting a letter would be an effort to establish them.

The Judge's Letter: Accepted for Movable Property, Not for Hudud
Ruling on the acceptance of the judge's letter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a judge's letter for movable property versus hudud and qisas, and explain the authority level of this ruling.

Core Idea
The judge's letter is accepted for movable property but not for fixed penalties, as per Imam Muhammad and the later scholars.

This lesson introduces the core ruling on the judge's letter (kitāb al-qāḍī) in Hanafi fiqh. While a judge's written communication is a valid form of evidence, its acceptance is restricted to cases involving transferable property (manqūl). It is not accepted for fixed penal punishments (ḥudūd) or retaliation (qiṣāṣ). This distinction is based on the principle of avoiding penalties in cases of doubt. The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) adopted this view from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is accepting a judge's written correspondence as legal evidence. The ruling is that it is accepted for movable property but not for hudud and qisas.
2
Locate the source and authority
The ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad in Zahir al-Riwayah. The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) followed this view, making it the relied-upon position.
3
Understand the rationale
The restriction for hudud and qisas is due to the principle of dropping penalties on doubt (shubha). The judge's letter introduces an element of uncertainty tolerable for property but not for severe punishments.
4
Note the authority weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and later confirmed by the mutā'akhkhirūn, giving it strong authority.
The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) adopted it, as stated in the text.

Concerning Muhammad: His acceptance of it (the judge's letter) in what is transferable, and the later scholars are upon this, not in a fixed penalty or retaliation. He writes a letter to the judge of Bukhara regarding that, and he makes two witnesses testify to his letter and his seal, and to what is in the letter. When the letter reaches the judge of Bukhara, and the witnesses testify that this is the letter of the judge of Samarkand and his seal, he orders the plaintiff to bring the witnesses who testified before him (the judge of Samarkand) first time, so that they testify in the presence of the slave that he is the property of this plaintiff. When they so testify, what does the judge of Bukhara do? The narrations from Abu Yusuf differ. It is mentioned in some narrations that the judge of Bukhara does not decide for the plaintiff regarding the slave, but writes another letter to the judge of Samarkand, writes in it what occurred before him, and makes two witnesses testify to his letter and his seal and what is in it, and sends the slave with him to Samarkand. When the two witnesses testify before him (the judge of Samarkand) to the letter and the seal and what is in the letter, and the justice of the witnesses is established, he decides for the plaintiff regarding the slave in the presence of the defendant, and discharges the guarantor of the plaintiff. In another narration: that the judge of Bukhara decides for the plaintiff regarding the slave, and writes to the judge of Samarkand so that the guarantor of the plaintiff is discharged. And upon the narration in which Abu Yusuf allowed the judge's letter in the matter of female slaves (for sexual relations), its form is what we mentioned regarding the slave, except that if the plaintiff is not trustworthy and reliable, then the judge written to does not hand her over to him, but orders the plaintiff to bring a trustworthy, reliable man in his religion and intellect, and sends her with him, because precaution in the matter of private parts is obligatory. This is in al-Muhit and other reliable works.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage addresses the acceptance of a judge's written correspondence (kitāb al-qāḍī) as legal evidence. Imam Muhammad states that such a letter is accepted for movable property (manqūl) but not for fixed penalties (ḥadd) or retaliation (qawad). The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) adopted this view. The text then illustrates with a case: a slave claimed by a plaintiff. The first judge writes to another judge. There is a dispute in the narrations from Abu Yusuf: in one, the second judge does not decide but writes back; in another, he decides directly. Additionally, Abu Yusuf allowed the letter in cases involving female slaves for sexual relations, but with precaution if the plaintiff is not trustworthy. The ruling is thus nuanced within the Hanafi school. The principal rule is that the judge's letter is a valid proof, but its application is restricted in severe punishments and sexual matters.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitāb al-qāḍī): A formal written communication from one judge to another containing testimony or a ruling.
حد وقود (ḥadd wa qawad): Fixed penal punishments (ḥudūd) and retaliation (qiṣāṣ).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) In hudud only
D) In qisas only

Key Takeaway
The judge's letter is a valid proof for property but not for hudud and qisas due to the principle of dropping penalties on doubt.

Procedural Dispute on the Judge's Letter: The Case of the Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the procedure for acting on the judge's letter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two narrations from Abu Yusuf regarding the procedure when a judge's letter is received and distinguish the ruling for female slaves.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf has two narrations on whether the second judge decides directly or writes back; for female slaves, extra precaution is needed if the plaintiff is untrustworthy.

This lesson delves into the practical application of the judge's letter through a detailed scenario involving a claimed slave. The text notes an intra-Hanafi dispute attributed to Abu Yusuf: one narration requires the second judge to write back to the first judge, while another allows him to decide directly. Additionally, when the letter concerns a female slave (for sexual relations), the receiving judge must ensure the plaintiff is trustworthy before handing her over. This illustrates how procedural caution interacts with substantive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A plaintiff claims a slave. The first judge writes a letter and sends witnesses. The letter reaches the second judge with testimony about the letter and its seal.
2
Identify the two narrations from Abu Yusuf
First narration: the second judge does not decide but writes back to the first judge. Second narration: the second judge decides directly in favor of the plaintiff.
Both narrations are attributed to Abu Yusuf, showing an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
In the first narration, the case is returned to the first judge for final decision; in the second, it is resolved locally. This affects the speed and location of the judgment.
4
Examine the special case of female slaves
If the letter concerns a female slave (istiḥlāl al-furūj), and the plaintiff is not trustworthy, the receiving judge does not hand her over but requires a reliable man to transport her. This is due to the obligatory precaution in matters of private parts.
The text cites al-Muhit for this rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن محمد قبوله فيما يُنْقَل وعليه المتأخرون لا في حد وقود. ويكتب كتاباً إلى قاضي بخارا بذلك ، ويشهد شاهدين على كتابه وختمه، وعلى ما في الكتاب، فإذا وصل الكتاب إلى قاضي بُخارا، وشهد الشهود أن هذا كتاب قاضي سمرقند وخاتمه ، أمر المدعي أن يحضر الشهود الذين شهدوا عنده أوّل مرة هذا، فيشهدون بحضرة العبد أنه ملك هذا المدعى، فإذا شهدوا بذلك ماذا يصنع قاضي بخارا ؟ اختلف الروايات عن أبي يوسف : ذكر في بعض الروايات أنَّ قاضي بخارا لا يقضي للمدعي بالعبد، ولكن يكتب كتاباً آخر إلى قاضى سَمَرْقَند ، ويكتب فيه ما جرى عنده ويشهد شاهدين على كتابه وختمه وما فيه، ويبعث بالعبدِ معه بسمرقند، وشهد الشاهدان عنده بالكتاب والختم وبما في الكتاب، وظهرت عدالة الشاهدين قضى للمدعي بالعبد بحضرة المدعى عليه، وأبرأ كفيل المدعي. وفي رواية : أن قاضي بُخارا يقضي بالعبد للمدعي، ويكتب إلى قاضي سَمَرْقَند حتى يبرأ كفيل المدعي. وعلى الرواية التي جوز أبو يوسف كتاب القاضي في الإماء، وصورته : ما ذكرنا في العبد غير أن المدعى إذا لم يكن ثقةً مأموناً ، فالقاضي المكتوب إليه لا يدفعها إليه، ولكن يأمر المدعي حتى يجيء برجل ثقة مأمون في دينه وعقله يبعث بها معه ؛ لأنَّ الاحتياط في باب الفروج واجب ، كذا في «المحيط» وغيره من المعتبرات.
Concerning Muhammad: His acceptance of it (the judge's letter) in what is transferable, and the later scholars are upon this, not in a fixed penalty or retaliation. He writes a letter to the judge of Bukhara regarding that, and he makes two witnesses testify to his letter and his seal, and to what is in the letter. When the letter reaches the judge of Bukhara, and the witnesses testify that this is the letter of the judge of Samarkand and his seal, he orders the plaintiff to bring the witnesses who testified before him (the judge of Samarkand) first time, so that they testify in the presence of the slave that he is the property of this plaintiff. When they so testify, what does the judge of Bukhara do? The narrations from Abu Yusuf differ. It is mentioned in some narrations that the judge of Bukhara does not decide for the plaintiff regarding the slave, but writes another letter to the judge of Samarkand, writes in it what occurred before him, and makes two witnesses testify to his letter and his seal and what is in it, and sends the slave with him to Samarkand. When the two witnesses testify before him (the judge of Samarkand) to the letter and the seal and what is in the letter, and the justice of the witnesses is established, he decides for the plaintiff regarding the slave in the presence of the defendant, and discharges the guarantor of the plaintiff. In another narration: that the judge of Bukhara decides for the plaintiff regarding the slave, and writes to the judge of Samarkand so that the guarantor of the plaintiff is discharged. And upon the narration in which Abu Yusuf allowed the judge's letter in the matter of female slaves (for sexual relations), its form is what we mentioned regarding the slave, except that if the plaintiff is not trustworthy and reliable, then the judge written to does not hand her over to him, but orders the plaintiff to bring a trustworthy, reliable man in his religion and intellect, and sends her with him, because precaution in the matter of private parts is obligatory. This is in al-Muhit and other reliable works.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage addresses the acceptance of a judge's written correspondence (kitāb al-qāḍī) as legal evidence. Imam Muhammad states that such a letter is accepted for movable property (manqūl) but not for fixed penalties (ḥadd) or retaliation (qawad). The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) adopted this view. The text then illustrates with a case: a slave claimed by a plaintiff. The first judge writes to another judge. There is a dispute in the narrations from Abu Yusuf: in one, the second judge does not decide but writes back; in another, he decides directly. Additionally, Abu Yusuf allowed the letter in cases involving female slaves for sexual relations, but with precaution if the plaintiff is not trustworthy. The ruling is thus nuanced within the Hanafi school. The principal rule is that the judge's letter is a valid proof, but its application is restricted in severe punishments and sexual matters.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitāb al-qāḍī): A formal written communication from one judge to another containing testimony or a ruling.
الإماء (al-imā’): Female slaves, specifically in the context of permitting sexual relations (istiḥlāl al-furūj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf's two narrations show the procedural flexibility in accepting the judge's letter, and extra caution applies for female slaves.

Conditions for the Judge's Letter: Reading, Sealing, and Handing Over with the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditions for validity of judge's letter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for the validity of a judge's letter according to the base Hanafi position, explain Abu Yusuf's objection, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The judge's letter is valid if witnesses testify it is his; reading, sealing, and handing over are not essential conditions.

This passage examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school: what exactly is required for a judge's written communication to be valid? The base text (al-Quduri) states three obligations: reading the letter to witnesses, sealing it in their presence, and handing it over to them. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed, arguing that none of these are necessary conditions. Later Hanafi authorities, including al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Nihayah, preferred Abu Yusuf's view. Understanding this dispute clarifies the flexibility in judicial procedures and the weight given to witness testimony over formalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three base conditions
From the Arabic text: 'يجب أن يقرأ على من يشهدهم، ويختم عندهم، ويسلم إليهم'. Note that the verb 'يجب' indicates these are obligatory acts for the judge who writes the letter.
These conditions aim to ensure the witnesses know the content and the letter is protected from tampering.
2
State Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf does not stipulate reading, sealing, or handing over to witnesses. His last view is that only making the witnesses testify that 'this is his book' is sufficient. Even the seal is not a condition.
His reasoning: the justice of the witnesses (their reliability) is the primary safeguard; formalities are secondary.
3
Understand the qiyas behind the base position
The base position analogizes from a general rule: 'لا شهادة بلا علم' (no testimony without knowledge). Just as witnesses must know the content of a debt document to testify, they must know the content of the judge's letter.
Abu Yusuf does not reject this principle; he holds that if the witnesses testify it is his book, that implies knowledge of its origin, even if they don't know the exact content.
4
Identify the mufta bihi and its practical implication
According to al-Nihayah and Shams al-A'imma, the practice today is to hand the letter to the plaintiff (following Abu Yusuf), and the seal is not a condition. The letter is valid as long as witnesses testify it is the judge's book.
Thus, reading and sealing are not mandatory for validity. The judge should still ideally do them, but omission does not invalidate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أن يقرأ على مَن يُشْهِدُهم ، ويختم عندهم، ويُسَلَّمَ إليهم، وأبو يوسف له لم يشترط شيئا من ذلك ، واختار الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له قوله) (١) ، فعند أبي يوسف الله يُشْهِدُهم أن هذا كتابه وختمه .... وعن أبي يوسف له الختم ليس بشرط. [١] قوله : ويجب ؛ على القاضي الكاتب أن يقرأ كتابه الذي كتبه إلى قاضي آخر ، على من يشهدهم ليعرفوا ما فيه، أو يعلمهم بما فيه بإخباره ؛ لأنه لا شهادة بلا علم المشهود به، كما لو شهدوا بأنَّ هذا الصك مكتوب على فلان لا يفيد ما لم يشهدوا بما تضمنه من الدين أو غيره، ويختم عندهم على الكتاب بعد طيه ؛ كيلا يتوهم التغير. وإنما قال : عندهم ؛ لأنه لا بُدَّ أن يشهدوا عنده أنَّ الخصم بحضرتهم، ويُسَلَّمَ القاضي ذلك الكتاب إليهم ؛ أي إلى هؤلاء الشهود في مجلس يصح حكمه فيه، فلو سلم في غير ذلك المجلس لم يصح، ذكره القُهُتَانِيَ له ناقلا عن الكِرْمَانِي . وقال في «النهاية»: عملُ القضاة اليومَ أنهم يُسلّمون المكتوب إلى المدعي، وهو قول أبي يوسف الله ، وهو اختيار الفتوى على قول شمس الأئمة ، وعلى قول أبي حنيفة الله يسلم المكتوب إلى الشهود. انتهى. [٢] قوله : لم يشترط شيئاً من ذلك ؛ المذكور سوى إشهادهم أن كتابه لما ابتلي بالقضاء، وهو قول أبي يوسف له آخراً. [٣] قوله : وعن أبي يوسف الله ؛ قال في «الفتح»(۲) : لا شك عندي في صحته، فإنَّ الفرض عدالة حملة الكتاب، فلا يضرُّ عدم ختمه مع شهادتهم أنه كتابه، نعم إذا كان الكتاب مع المدعي ينبغي اشتراط الختم ؛ لاحتمال التغيير، إلا أن يشهدوا بما فيه حفظاً. ا ا. انتهى.
It is obligatory that he reads it to those whom he makes witnesses, seals it before them, and hands it over to them. Abu Yusuf did not stipulate any of that. Imam al-Sarakhsi chose his saying. So according to Abu Yusuf, he makes them testify that this is his letter and his seal... And from Abu Yusuf: the seal is not a condition. [1] Commentary: It is obligatory for the writing judge to read his book to those he makes witnesses so that they know what is in it, or inform them by telling them, because there is no testimony without knowledge of the subject matter, as if they testify that this document is written on so-and-so, it is not useful unless they testify to what it contains of debt or other things. And he seals it before them after folding it so that tampering is not suspected. And he said 'before them' because it is necessary that they testify in his presence that the opponent is in their presence. And the judge hands over that book to them, i.e., to these witnesses in a session in which his judgment is valid. If he hands over in a different session, it is not valid, as mentioned by al-Quhistani quoting al-Kirmani. And it is said in al-Nihayah: The practice of judges today is that they hand over the writing to the plaintiff, which is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and it is the choice for fatwa according to Shams al-A'imma. And according to Abu Hanifa, he hands over the writing to the witnesses. [2] Commentary: He did not stipulate any of that mentioned except making them testify that his book is due to his being afflicted with the judiciary, and this is the last saying of Abu Yusuf. [3] Commentary: And from Abu Yusuf: He said in al-Fath: There is no doubt in my mind about its validity, because the presumed condition is the justice of the bearers of the book, so the absence of a seal does not harm when they testify that it is his book. Yes, if the book is with the plaintiff, the seal should be required due to the possibility of tampering, unless they testify to its content from memory.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage details the conditions for the validity of a judge's written communication. The base text states that the judge must read the letter to the witnesses, seal it in their presence, and hand it to them. However, Abu Yusuf does not require these conditions. Imam al-Sarakhsi preferred Abu Yusuf's view. The commentary explains the reasoning: testimony requires knowledge, so the witnesses must know the content. Sealing prevents tampering. Handing over must be in the same session. However, later practice, as in al-Nihayah, is to hand over to the plaintiff, following Abu Yusuf. There is also a discussion whether the seal is necessary; Abu Yusuf's last view is that it is not a condition. The relied-upon position is that the letter is valid without seal if witnesses testify it is the judge's book, but if the letter is with the plaintiff, seal is recommended.

Key Terms
يقرأ (yaqra'): The judge reads the letter aloud to the witnesses so they know its contents.
يختم (yakhtum): The judge seals the letter after folding it, in the presence of witnesses, to prevent tampering.
يسلم (yusallim): The judge hands over the letter to the witnesses or to the plaintiff.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To prevent the plaintiff from seeing it
D) To make the letter official

Key Takeaway
The judge's letter is valid if witnesses testify it is his book; reading, sealing, and handing over are not essential conditions according to the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf.

Obligations of the Judge Writing a Judicial Letter
Ruling on the requirements for writing and dispatching a judicial letter to another judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three obligations of a writing judge according to Abu Hanifa, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A judge's letter requires witnesses to know its contents and attest to its authenticity; Abu Yusuf's relaxed view is the operative practice.

When a judge writes to another judge, the transmission must verify the letter's authenticity. Abu Hanifa imposes three specific duties: reading the letter to witnesses, sealing it in their presence, and handing it to them. Abu Yusuf argues these are unnecessary, and later Hanafi practice adopts his position. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three wajib actions according to Abu Hanifa
From the text: the judge must read the letter to the witnesses, seal it in their presence, and hand the sealed letter to them in a valid judicial session.
These are found in the phrase 'wa yajib an yaqraʼa ... wa yakhtimu ... wa yusallimu'.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil) behind each
Reading ensures witnesses have direct knowledge (testimony requires knowledge). Sealing prevents suspicion of alteration. Handing to witnesses (not plaintiff) ensures they can later testify: the letter was authentic and received in the proper session.
The text explicitly links reading to 'la shahada bi-la ilm'.
3
Compare with Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf required only the witnesses' testimony that it is the judge's letter; reading and sealing are not conditions. His later view also dropped the seal requirement, relying on the carriers' reliability.
The text states 'Abu Yusuf lam yashtarit shayʼan min dhalik'.
4
Apply the tarjih (preferred view)
Imam al-Sarakhsi and later scholars chose Abu Yusuf's position as the mufta bihi. In practice, judges hand the document to the plaintiff directly, not to witnesses.
The text says 'wa huwa ikhtiyar al-fatwa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أن يقرأ على مَن يُشْهِدُهم، ويختم عندهم، ويُسَلِّم إليهم، وأبو يوسف له لم يشترط شيئا من ذلك، واختار الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له قولَهُ [۱] قوله : ويجب ؛ على القاضي الكاتب أن يقرأ كتابه الذي كتبه إلى قاضي آخر ، على من يشهدهم ليعرفوا ما فيه، أو يعلمهم بما فيه بإخباره ؛ لأنه لا شهادة بلا علم المشهود به، كما لو شهدوا بأنَّ هذا الصك مكتوب على فلان لا يفيد ما لم يشهدوا بما تضمنه من الدين أو غيره، ويختم عندهم على الكتاب بعد طيه ؛ كيلا يتوهم التغير. وإنما قال : عندهم ؛ لأنه لا بُدَّ أن يشهدوا عنده أنَّ الخصم بحضرتهم، ويُسَلَّمَ القاضي ذلك الكتاب إليهم ؛ أي إلى هؤلاء الشهود في مجلس يصح حكمه فيه، فلو سلم في غير ذلك المجلس لم يصح، ذكره القُهُتَانِيَ له ناقلا عن الكِرْمَانِي . وقال في «النهاية»: عملُ القضاة اليومَ أنهم يُسلّمون المكتوب إلى المدعي، وهو قول أبي يوسف الله ، وهو اختيار الفتوى على قول شمس الأئمة ، وعلى قول أبي حنيفة الله يسلم المكتوب إلى الشهود. انتهى. [٢] قوله : لم يشترط شيئاً من ذلك ؛ المذكور سوى إشهادهم أن كتابه لما ابتلي بالقضاء، وهو قول أبي يوسف له آخراً. [٣] قوله : وعن أبي يوسف الله ؛ قال في «الفتح»(۲) : لا شك عندي في صحته، فإنَّ الفرض عدالة حملة الكتاب، فلا يضرُّ عدم ختمه مع شهادتهم أنه كتابه، نعم إذا كان الكتاب مع المدعي ينبغي اشتراط الختم ؛ لاحتمال التغيير، إلا أن يشهدوا بما فيه حفظاً. ا ا. انتهى. وعن أبي يوسف له الختم ليس بشرط.
It is obligatory that he read it to those whom he makes witnesses, and seal it in their presence, and hand it over to them. Abu Yusuf, may Allah be pleased with him, did not require any of that, and Imam al-Sarakhsi chose his statement. [1] His statement: 'It is obligatory' – the writing judge must read his book that he wrote to another judge to those whom he makes witnesses so that they know what is in it, or inform them of its contents by telling him; because there is no testimony without knowledge of the testified matter, just as if they testify that this document is written against so-and-so, it is not beneficial unless they testify to what it contains of debt or other things. And he seals it in their presence after folding it, so that alteration is not suspected. He only said 'in their presence' because they must testify in his presence that the opponent is in their presence. And the judge hands that book over to them, i.e., to these witnesses in a session where his ruling is valid; if he hands it over in a different session, it is not valid, as mentioned by al-Quhustani quoting al-Kirmani. And he said in al-Nihayah: The practice of judges today is that they hand over the written document to the plaintiff, which is the statement of Abu Yusuf, and it is the choice for fatwa according to Shams al-A'imma, and according to Abu Hanifa the written document is handed over to the witnesses. End. [2] His statement: 'did not require any of that' – mentioned except their testimony that it is his book when he was tested with judiciary, and this is the later statement of Abu Yusuf. [3] His statement: 'And from Abu Yusuf' – he said in al-Fath: There is no doubt in my opinion about its validity, for the assumption is the reliability of the carriers of the book, so the absence of his seal does not harm along with their testimony that it is his book. Yes, if the book is with the plaintiff, the seal should be required due to the possibility of alteration, unless they testify to its contents from memory. End. And from Abu Yusuf, the seal is not a condition.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the obligations of a judge when writing a judicial letter to another judge. The Hanafi position of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the writing judge must perform three actions: 1) read the letter to the witnesses so they know its contents (since testimony requires knowledge), 2) seal it in their presence to prevent tampering, and 3) hand the sealed letter to the witnesses (not the plaintiff) in the session where his ruling is valid. The commentary explains the rationale: testimony requires knowledge of the subject matter; sealing prevents suspicion of alteration; handing to witnesses ensures they can testify later. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, requiring only that the witnesses testify that it is the judge's letter; he considers reading and sealing unnecessary. The later scholars, including Imam al-Sarakhsi, prefer Abu Yusuf's position, and the fatwa is upon his view, as indicated by the practice of judges handing the document to the plaintiff directly. Even regarding the seal, one transmission from Abu Yusuf states it is not a condition, supported by the reliability of the letter-bearers. However, if the letter is with the plaintiff, sealing is advisable to guard against alteration.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of making someone a witness; here, the judge makes witnesses hear the contents of the letter.
ختم (khatm): Sealing the letter with the judge's seal to prevent alteration.
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written judgment or letter from one judge to another, binding on the recipient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because testimony requires knowledge of the testified matter
C) To allow the witnesses to suggest corrections
D) To prove the judge is literate

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi position for judicial letters is that only witnessing the letter's authenticity is required; reading and sealing are not obligatory, as per Abu Yusuf's view chosen by later scholars.

Conditions for Accepting a Judicial Letter by the Receiving Judge
Ruling on requirements for accepting a judicial letter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge accepts a judicial letter from another judge and distinguish between binding and non-binding letters.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is only accepted in the presence of the opponent and with testimony of two witnesses, unless the opponent admits its authenticity.

This passage explains the rigorous requirements for authenticating a written judgment from one judge to another. Unlike non-binding communications, a judicial letter carries legal force and thus demands strict verification. The commentator contrasts this with letters requesting safe conduct or verifying character, which do not require such proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main conditions
From the text, note that the receiving judge does not accept the letter except in the presence of the opponent (khasm) and with testimony of two men or a man and two women.
Also note the seal condition: if letter is with plaintiff, seal required; if with witnesses, not required.
2
Understand the seal condition variation
The commentator states: 'If the book is in the hand of the plaintiff, it is ruled that the seal is a condition; if in the hand of the witnesses, it is not a condition.' Reflect on why witnesses substitute for the seal.
The witnesses' testimony provides authenticity, making the seal redundant.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a difference on whether the opponent's presence is a condition for accepting the letter itself or for accepting the evidence against the writer. The main view (al-Ikhtiyar) requires it for the letter; another view (al-Dhakhira) for the evidence.
This difference affects when the letter can be received.
4
Contrast with non-binding letters
The text contrasts with letters from enemy territory asking for aman (safe conduct) and letters between judge and character verifier (muzakki). These are not binding, so no testimony is required.
Binding nature (ilzam) is the key factor requiring proof.
5
Apply the admission exception
Testimony is only needed if the opponent denies the letter is from the judge. If he admits it, witnesses are unnecessary. This is a key exception.
Admission eliminates the need for external proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : إذا كان الكتابُ في يدِ المدَّعي يُفْتَى بأنَّ الختم شرطٌ ، وإِن كانَ في يَدِ الشُّهودِ يُفْتَى بأنَّه ليس بشرط. (وإذا سَلَّمَ إلى المكتوب إليه لم يقبله إلا بحضرة خصمه وبشهادة الرجلين، أو رجل وامرأتين، فإذا شهدوا أنه كتاب قاضي فلان ومر أنه روي عن أبي يوسف له أنه قال : إن كان الكتاب منشوراً يقبلُهُ القاضي فهاهنا أولى. [1] قوله : لم يقبله...الخ ؛ أي لا يأخذ القاضي الكتاب إلا وقت حضور الخصم ؛ لأنه لإلزامه، كما في «الاختيار»، لكن في «الذخيرة»، وغيرها : إِنَّ حضور الخصم شرط قبول البينة على الكاتب، لا شرط قبول الكتاب كما سبقت الإشارة إليه. [2] قوله : وبشهادة ...الخ ؛ لأنَّ الكتاب يشبه الكتاب، فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة ، وهي البينة ؛ وهذا لأنَّ كتاب القاضي ملزم، فلا بُدَّ للملزم من الحجة حتى يثبت بها كونه ملزماً. بخلاف ما إذا جاء من ملك أهل الحرب في طلب الأمان فإنه مقبول بغير بينة ، حتى لو أمنه الإمامُ صح ؛ لأنَّ هذا الكتاب ليس بملزم ، فإنَّ الإمام بالخيار إن شاء أعطاه الأمان، وإن شاء لم يعطه، فلا يشترط البينة. وبخلاف رسول القاضي إلى المزكّي، ورسول المزكِّي إلى القاضي ؛ لأنَّ الإلزام على المدعى عليه بالشهادة لا بالتزكية، حتى لو قضى القاضي بالشهادة بدون التزكية صح قضاؤه ، فإنَّ التزكية إنما تكون لنوع رجحان الصدق. كذا في شروح الهداية. واحتياج الشهادة إذا أنكر الخصم كونَه كتاب القاضي، أما إذا أقر فلا حاجة إلى الشهود. كذا «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. [3] قوله : فإذا شهدوا ؛ ولا بُدَّ من إسلامهم، ولو كان لذمّي على ذمي ؛ لأنهم
I say: If the book is in the hand of the plaintiff, it is ruled that the seal is a condition; and if it is in the hand of the witnesses, it is ruled that it is not a condition. (And when it is handed over to the addressee, he does not accept it except in the presence of his opponent and with the testimony of two men, or a man and two women. When they testify that it is the letter of so-and-so judge... and it has been narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: If the letter is unsealed, the judge accepts it; then here it is more appropriate. [1] His statement: 'he does not accept it...' – meaning the judge does not take the letter except at the time of the opponent's presence, because it is for his binding, as in al-Ikhtiyar. But in al-Dhakhira and others: the presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence against the writer, not a condition for accepting the letter, as was indicated earlier. [2] His statement: 'and with testimony...' – because one letter resembles another, so it is only established by a complete proof, which is evidence. This is because the judge's letter is binding, so the binder must have proof to establish its binding nature. Unlike if someone comes from the land of war asking for safety, it is accepted without evidence, even if the Imam grants him safety, it is valid; because this letter is not binding, for the Imam has the choice: if he wishes, he grants safety, and if he wishes, he does not, so evidence is not required. And unlike the messenger of the judge to the verifier (muzakki) and the messenger of the verifier to the judge, because the binding is on the defendant by testimony, not by verification; even if the judge judges by testimony without verification, his judgment is valid, for verification is only for a type of preponderance of truthfulness. This is as in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. And the need for testimony is when the opponent denies that it is the judge's letter; but if he admits, there is no need for witnesses. As in Majma' al-Anhur and others. [3] His statement: 'When they testify' – and they must be Muslims, even if it is a dhimmi against a dhimmi; because they...
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage deals with the conditions under which a judge accepts a written judgment from another judge. First, the commentator notes a practical fatwa: if the letter is in the plaintiff's hands, sealing is required to prevent alteration; if in the witnesses' hands, seal is not required because the witnesses' testimony suffices. The main ruling: the receiving judge does not accept the letter unless the opponent (defendant) is present, and two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses testify that it is the letter of the issuing judge. The commentary explains that the opponent's presence is necessary because the letter is binding on him; however, some authorities say it is a condition for accepting the evidence against the writer, not for accepting the letter itself. The requirement of testimony is because letters resemble one another, so full evidence (bayyinah) is needed to establish authenticity and binding nature. This contrasts with non-binding letters, such as a letter from enemy territory requesting aman (safe conduct) – such a letter is accepted without evidence because the Imam is not bound; he may accept or reject. Similarly, letters between a judge and a character verifier (muzakki) are not binding because they relate to verification, not direct judgment. The need for testimony arises only if the opponent denies the letter; if he admits it, witnesses are unnecessary. Additionally, the witnesses must be Muslims, even if both parties are dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects).

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal proof, specifically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
خصم (khasm): The opponent (defendant) against whom the letter is binding.
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written judgment or order from a judge to another judge, binding if properly authenticated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the seal of a judicial letter considered not necessary?
A) When the letter is in the hands of the plaintiff
B) When the letter is in the hands of the witnesses
C) When the opponent admits the letter's authenticity
D) When the letter comes from enemy territory

Key Takeaway
A judicial letter is only accepted if the opponent is present and its authenticity is established by testimony, unless the opponent admits it.

Conditions for Accepting a Judge's Letter
Ruling on procedure for judge's letter with proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting a judge's letter and explain the Hanafi ruling on the effect of the writer's death.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is accepted only with opponent present and witnesses; its seal is conditional, and the letter becomes invalid if the writer dies.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya lays out the procedural requirements for a receiving judge to act upon a letter from another judge. It also addresses a critical dispute: does the letter survive the writer's death? The relied-upon Hanafi position is that it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
State the three procedural conditions: presence of opponent, testimony of two men (or man+two women), and the seal condition which depends on who holds the letter (required if plaintiff holds it, not required if witnesses hold it).
The presence of opponent is for accepting evidence on the writer, not for accepting the letter itself.
2
Understand the rationale for conditions
Because the letter is binding, it requires complete evidence (bayyinah). Non-binding letters, like an amnesty request, do not require evidence.
The letter from enemy territory is not binding on the Imam.
3
Analyze the death dispute
The relied-upon Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) holds the letter becomes invalid upon the writer's death. Abu Yusuf and other schools hold it remains valid, analogizing to secondary witnesses.
The opposing analogy: judge as secondary witness vs. judge as originator.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
Mufta bihi: letter becomes invalid; if the writing judge dies before the receiving judge rules, the case must be re-litigated.
This is the position in the Hidayah and its commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا سَلَّمَ إلى المكتوب إليه لم يقبله إلا بحضرة خصمه وبشهادة الرجلين، أو رجل وامرأتين، فإذا شهدوا أنه كتاب قاضي فلان
أقول : إذا كان الكتابُ في يدِ المدَّعي يُفْتَى بأنَّ الختم شرطٌ ، وإِن كانَ في يَدِ الشُّهودِ يُفْتَى بأنَّه ليس بشرط.
(وإذا سَلَّمَ إلى المكتوب إليه لم يقبله إلا بحضرة خصمه وبشهادة الرجلين، أو رجل وامرأتين، فإذا شهدوا" أنه كتاب قاضي فلان ومر أنه روي عن أبي يوسف له أنه قال : إن كان الكتاب منشوراً يقبلُهُ القاضي فهاهنا أولى. 11 ] قوله : لم يقبله...الخ ؛ أي لا يأخذ القاضي الكتاب إلا وقت حضور الخصم ؛ لأنه لإلزامه، كما في «الاختيار ) ، لكن في «الذخيرة»، وغيرها : إِنَّ حضور الخصم شرط قبول البينة على الكاتب، لا شرط قبول الكتاب كما سبقت الإشارة إليه.
[٢) قوله : وبشهادة ...الخ ؛ لأنَّ الكتاب يشبه الكتاب، فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة ، وهي البينة ؛ وهذا لأنَّ كتاب القاضي ملزم، فلا بُدَّ للملزم من الحجة حتى يثبت بها كونه ملزماً.

[3]قوله : فإذا شهدوا ؛ ولا بُدَّ من إسلامهم، ولو كان لذمّي على ذمي ؛ لأنهم يشهدون على فعل المسلم، وهو أنَّ القاضي كتب الكتاب، وختمه وقرأه عليهم، وسلّمه إليهم ولم يشترط في الكتاب ظهور العدالة للفتح.
والصحيح أنه يفض الكتاب بعد ثبوت العدالة، كذا ذكره الخصاف له ؛ لأنَّ العدالة متى لم تظهر يحتاج إلى أن يزيد في شهوده ؛ لعدم ظهور عدالة هؤلاء، وإنما يمكنهم أداء الشهادة بعد قيام الختم ، فيشهدون أنّ هذا كتاب فلان القاضي وختمه ، وأما إذا فك الخاتم فلا يقدرون على ذلك. (1) قوله : وسلّمه إلينا : هذا شرط للحكم به، حتى إذا قالوا : لم يسلّمه إلينا، أو لم يقرأه علينا ، أو لم يختمه بحضرتنا لم يعمل به. كذا في «التبيين»). [٢] قوله : وألزمه ما فيه ؛ يعني إذا ثبت عدالتهم عنده بأن كان يعرفهم بالعدالة ، أو وجد في الكتاب عدالتهم، أو كان القاضي الكاتب قد كتب عدالتهم، أو سأل من يعرفهم من الثقات، فزكوا ، أما قبل ظهور عدالتهم فلا يحكم به، ولا يلزم الخصم ؛ لأنه لا يكون حجة إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم. صرَّح به الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز» (۳). [٣]قوله : فيبطل ... الخ ؛ وقال أبو يوسف له : لا يبطل، بل المكتوب إليه يقضي ، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد الله في رواية. كذا في «الرمز» (۳). لهم : إنَّ القاضي الكاتب بمنزلة أداء شهودِ الفرع الشهادة ؛ لأنه ينقل شهادة الذين شهدوا بالحق إلى القاضي المكتوب إليه ، والنقل قد تم بالكتابة ، فصار بمنزلة شهودِ الفرع إذا ماتوا بعد أداء الشهادةِ قبل القضاء بها، فإنّه لا يمنع القضاء، فكذا هذا، وهكذا الحكم في كلِّ شاهد مات بعد أداء الشهادة قبل الحكم بها.
And when he [the sender] delivers [the letter] to the addressee, he [the receiving judge] does not accept it except in the presence of his [the opponent's] opponent and with the testimony of two men, or a man and two women. Then if they testify that it is the letter of judge so-and-so... I say: If the letter is in the hand of the plaintiff, the fatwa is given that the seal is a condition, and if it is in the hand of the witnesses, the fatwa is given that it is not a condition. (And when he delivers to the addressee, he does not accept it except in the presence of his opponent and with the testimony of two men, or a man and two women. Then if they testify that it is the letter of judge so-and-so... and it has passed that it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: If the letter is unsealed, the judge accepts it; so here it is more appropriate. His statement: 'he does not accept it...' i.e., the judge does not take the letter except at the time of the opponent's presence because it is for binding him, as in al-Ikhtiyar. But in al-Dhakhira and others: The presence of the opponent is a condition for accepting the evidence regarding the writer, not a condition for accepting the letter, as previously indicated. [2] His statement: 'and with the testimony...' because the letter resembles a document, so it is not established except with a complete proof, which is evidence. This is because the judge's letter is binding, so the binding thing must have evidence to establish it as binding. Unlike when someone comes from the land of war seeking amnesty, he is accepted without evidence, even if the Imam grants him amnesty it is valid, because this letter is not binding, for the Imam has the option: if he wishes he gives him amnesty, if he wishes he does not. So evidence is not required. And unlike the messenger of the judge to the character witness, and the messenger of the character witness to the judge, because the binding upon the defendant is by the testimony, not by the character verification. Even if the judge rules based on testimony without character verification, his ruling is valid, for character verification is only for a certain preponderance of truthfulness. This is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. The need for testimony is when the opponent denies that it is the judge's letter; but if he admits, there is no need for witnesses. 'Majma' al-Anhur' and others. [3] His statement: 'then if they testify' and they must be Muslims, even if it is for a dhimmi against a dhimmi, because they testify about the act of a Muslim, namely that the judge wrote the letter, sealed it, read it to them, and handed it to them. And it is not required in the letter that the witnesses' uprightness be apparent for opening. The correct view is that the letter is opened after the establishment of uprightness, as al-Khassaf mentioned for him, because when uprightness is not apparent, he needs to increase his witnesses due to the non-appearance of their uprightness. They can only perform the testimony after the seal is intact, so they testify that this is the letter of so-and-so judge and his seal. But if the seal is broken, they cannot do that. (1) His statement: 'and he handed it to us': this is a condition for ruling by it, such that if they say: he did not hand it to us, or he did not read it to us, or he did not seal it in our presence, it is not acted upon. This is in al-Tabyin. [2] His statement: 'and he makes binding what is in it' means when their uprightness is established before him, whether he knows them for uprightness, or he finds their uprightness in the letter, or the writing judge wrote their uprightness, or he asked those whom he trusts and they attested to their uprightness. But before the appearance of their uprightness, he does not rule by it, nor does he bind the opponent, because it is not a proof except after the appearance of their uprightness. This is stated by al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz. [3] His statement: 'it becomes invalid...' Abu Yusuf said: it does not become invalid; rather the addressee judge judges by it, and that is the saying of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration. This is in al-Ramz. Their proof: the writing judge is like the performance of testimony by secondary witnesses, because he transmits the testimony of those who testified to the right to the addressee judge, and the transmission has been completed by the writing, so it becomes like secondary witnesses when they die after performing testimony before judgment is passed by it; that does not prevent judgment, so likewise here. And thus is the ruling for every witness who dies after performing testimony before judgment is passed by it.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a supercommentary (hashiya) excerpt from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, explaining the rules for a judge's letter sent to another judge (Kitab al-Qadi ila al-Qadi). The base text (from al-Hidayah) states that the receiving judge does not accept the letter except in the presence of the opponent and with the testimony of two men or a man and two women. The commentator first notes a fatwa difference regarding the seal: if the letter is in the plaintiff's hand, the seal is required; if in the witnesses' hand, it is not. Then he explains that the condition of the opponent's presence is for accepting the evidence on the writer, not for accepting the letter itself. The need for witnesses is because the letter is binding, unlike a letter from enemy territory seeking amnesty, which is not binding. Also, the testimony is only needed if the opponent denies it is the judge's letter; if he admits, no witnesses are needed. The witnesses must be Muslim, even in cases involving dhimmis, because they testify about the act of a Muslim (the judge writing and sealing). The letter is opened only after the witnesses' uprightness is established. Finally, the passage addresses the effect of the writer's death: according to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), it becomes invalid; Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold it does not, analogizing the judge to secondary witnesses who have already performed testimony.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (Kitāb al-Qāḍī ilā al-Qāḍī): A written letter from one judge to another containing a ruling or order to be executed in the addressee's jurisdiction.
بينة (Bayyinah): Formal legal evidence, here specifically testimony of witnesses (two men or a man and two women) to verify the letter's authenticity.
ختم (Khatm): The seal on the judge's letter, which serves as a mark of authenticity. Its necessity depends on who holds the letter.
شهود الفرع (Shuhūd al-Far‘): Secondary witnesses who testify based on the testimony of primary witnesses; used in analogy for the judge's transmission of testimony via letter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to a judge's letter if the writing judge dies before the receiving judge rules?
A) The letter becomes invalid and cannot be acted upon.
B) The letter remains valid and the receiving judge can rule by it.
C) The letter is void only if the receiving judge also dies.
D) The letter is referred back to the original court.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter requires strict procedural conditions; the most debated is whether the writer's death invalidates the letter (Hanafi says yes, others say no).
