Conditions for a Judge's Written Judgment (Kitab al-Qadi)
Specifies conditions for a judge's written order to another judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid written judgment from one Hanafi judge to another and explain how its enforcement depends on witness integrity and seal integrity.

Core Idea
A written judgment is valid only through specific procedural acts and witness integrity; seal and testimony are conditions of authenticity.

The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya details the strict procedural conditions for a judge's written order (kitab al-qadi). Unlike ordinary letters, this judicial document requires a formal process: the judge must write, read aloud in court, seal, and hand over the document to witnesses. Their testimony, while the seal is intact, establishes the document's authenticity. The receiving judge cannot enforce the judgment until he verifies the witnesses' integrity (adala). This system ensures reliability in inter-judicial communication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural sequence
The writing judge must: (1) write the judgment, (2) read it aloud in his court, (3) seal it, and (4) hand it to the witnesses. These acts are conditions for the witnesses' testimony.
The witnesses testify to these specific actions, not the content of the judgment.
2
Recognize the role of the seal
The witnesses testify while the seal is intact. If the seal is broken before testimony, they cannot confirm the document's authenticity, invalidating their testimony.
The seal is the physical proof that the document has not been tampered with.
3
Understand the condition of witness integrity
The receiving judge must establish the witnesses' adala (integrity) before opening and enforcing the book. This can be done through personal knowledge, written certification from the writing judge, or inquiry from trustworthy sources.
Without adala, the book is not a binding proof (hujja).
4
Note the effect of the judge's death
If the writing judge dies before the book is acted upon, the book becomes void. The receiving judge cannot enforce it because the authority of the writing judge has ceased.
The book is only valid as long as the writer remains a judge.
5
Summarize the enforcement conditions
The receiving judge enforces only after verifying the witnesses' adala. The enforcement involves reading the book to the defendant and compelling compliance.
The procedure ensures both authenticity and fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرأه علينا في محكمته وختمه وسلمه إلينا، فتح القاضي وقرأ على الخصم وألزمه ما فيه إن بقي كاتبه قاضياً، فيبطل بموته
يشهدون على فعل المسلم، وهو أنَّ القاضي كتب الكتاب، وختمه وقرأه عليهم، وسلّمه إليهم ولم يشترط في الكتاب ظهور العدالة للفتح. والصحيح أنه يفض الكتاب بعد ثبوت العدالة، كذا ذكره الخصاف له؛ لأنَّ العدالة متى لم تظهر يحتاج إلى أن يزيد في شهوده؛ لعدم ظهور عدالة هؤلاء، وإنما يمكنهم أداء الشهادة بعد قيام الختم، فيشهدون أنّ هذا كتاب فلان القاضي وختمه، وأما إذا فك الخاتم فلا يقدرون على ذلك. قوله: وسلّمه إلينا: هذا شرط للحكم به، حتى إذا قالوا: لم يسلّمه إلينا، أو لم يقرأه علينا، أو لم يختمه بحضرتنا لم يعمل به. كذا في «التبيين». قوله: وألزمه ما فيه؛ يعني إذا ثبت عدالتهم عنده بأن كان يعرفهم بالعدالة، أو وجد في الكتاب عدالتهم، أو كان القاضي الكاتب قد كتب عدالتهم، أو سأل من يعرفهم من الثقات، فزكوا، أما قبل ظهور عدالتهم فلا يحكم به، ولا يلزم الخصم؛ لأنه لا يكون حجة إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم. صرَّح به الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز».
He read it to us in his court, sealed it, and handed it to us. Then the judge opened it, read it to the defendant, and enforced what was in it as long as its writer remained a judge; it becomes void upon his death. They testify to the action of a Muslim, which is that the judge wrote the book, sealed it, read it to them, and handed it to them. In the book, the appearance of integrity was not stipulated for opening. The correct view is that the book is opened after the establishment of integrity, as mentioned by al-Khassaf for him, because when integrity does not appear, he needs to increase in his witnesses due to the non-appearance of their integrity. They can only perform testimony after the seal remains intact, so they testify that this is the book of so-and-so the judge and his seal. But if the seal is broken, they cannot do that. His statement: 'and handed it to us': this is a condition for acting upon it, so that if they say: he did not hand it to us, or he did not read it to us, or he did not seal it in our presence, it is not acted upon. This is in 'al-Tabyin'. His statement: 'and enforced what was in it' means that if their integrity is established with him, whether by his knowing them to be upright, or finding their integrity written in the book, or the writing judge having recorded their integrity, or asking those who know them from trustworthy people and they praised them, but before the appearance of their integrity he does not judge by it and does not compel the defendant, because it is not a proof except after the appearance of their integrity. This was stated by al-Zayla'i in 'Sharh al-Kanz'.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is on the chapter of judicial procedure, specifically the conditions for a written judgment sent from one judge to another. The base text (matn) states the procedure: the writing judge reads the judgment in his court, seals it, and hands it to witnesses. The receiving judge then opens it, reads it to the defendant, and enforces it as long as the writing judge remains in office; it becomes void upon his death. The commentary explains that the witnesses must testify to these acts. The correct position is that the book is not opened until the integrity (adala) of the witnesses is established, because without integrity the testimony is weak. The witnesses can only testify while the seal is intact; if the seal is broken, they cannot confirm it is the judge's book. The passage also clarifies that the handing over, reading, and sealing in the witnesses' presence are conditions for acting on the book. Finally, the receiving judge must verify the witnesses' integrity through various means; only then does the book become a binding proof against the defendant. All explanations are from Hanafi sources like al-Tabyin and al-Zayla'i's Sharh al-Kanz.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written judgment or order from one judge to another, containing a ruling based on testimony.
ختم (khatm): Seal; the judge's personal seal affixed to the written judgment.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony; the witnesses' attestation to the judge's acts of writing, sealing, reading aloud, and handing over the book.
عدالة (adalah): Integrity; the required moral probity of witnesses enabling their testimony to be accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the witnesses to the judge's written judgment testify after the seal is broken?
A) Their testimony is accepted on the basis of their integrity.
B) Their testimony is not accepted because they cannot confirm the seal.
C) The receiving judge can still open the book and proceed.
D) The witnesses must re-testify before a different judge.

Key Takeaway
A judge's written judgment is only enforceable when the procedural acts are witnessed, the seal intact, and the witnesses' integrity established.

Effect of the Writing Judge's Death or Dismissal
Dispute on validity of a judge's written judgment after his death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the effect of a writing judge's death or dismissal on his written judgment, explain the opposing view of Abu Yusuf and other imams, and evaluate the proofs for each side, including the exception when the book has been read.

Core Idea
A judge's written order is an incomplete judgment — death voids it; testimony is different.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a practical problem: if a judge writes a judgment and transmits it to another judge but dies before it is executed, does the document remain binding? The Hanafi school, represented by Abu Hanifa, holds that it becomes void, while Abu Yusuf and several other imams consider it still enforceable. The dispute turns on whether the judge's conveyance is treated as a judgment or as testimony.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa (void upon death/dismissal before reading) and the ruling of Abu Yusuf (remains valid). Note that Abu Yusuf's view is shared by al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf — the practical difference.
2
Identify the opposing proof
Understand Abu Yusuf's analogy: the writing judge is like branch witnesses (shuhud al-far') who convey testimony; once written, the conveyance is complete, so death does not affect it.
The opposition argues that the obligation on the receiving judge is already established by the writing.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the Hanafi response: the conveyance has the effect of a judgment, not mere testimony, because it is only valid from a judge, does not require number or wording, and is obligatory upon hearing evidence. As a judgment, it is incomplete until the receiving judge reads it, so death voids it.
Key distinction: judgment vs. testimony.
4
Note the exception
If the writing judge dies after the book has been read by the receiving judge, the book remains valid because the judgment was established by reading.
Al-Fath clarifies that the relevant moment is reading, not arrival.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the receiving judge cannot enforce the judgment if the writing judge dies before reading; under Abu Yusuf, he can. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
This affects whether a new trial is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فيبطل ... الخ؛ وقال أبو يوسف له: لا يبطل، بل المكتوب إليه يقضي، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية. كذا في «الرمز». لهم: إنَّ القاضي الكاتب بمنزلة أداء شهودِ الفرع الشهادة؛ لأنه ينقل شهادة الذين شهدوا بالحق إلى القاضي المكتوب إليه، والنقل قد تم بالكتابة، فصار بمنزلة شهودِ الفرع إذا ماتوا بعد أداء الشهادةِ قبل القضاء بها، فإنّه لا يمنع القضاء، فكذا هذا، وهكذا الحكم في كلِّ شاهد مات بعد أداء الشهادة قبل الحكم بها.
ولنا: إنَّ القاضي الكاتب وإن كان ينقل شهادة الذين شهدوا عنده إلا أنَّ لهذا النقل حكم القضاء، ألا ترى أنَّ هذا النقل لا يصح إلا من القاضي، ولا يشترط فيه عدده، ولا لفظ الشهادة، ووجب على القاضي الكاتب هذا النقل بسماع البينة. وما يجب على القاضي بسماع البينة قضاءً فثبت أن لهذا النقل حكم القضاء ولم يتم بعد؛ لأنَّ تمامه يوجب القضاء على المكتوب إليه، ولا يجب القضاء على المكتوب إليه قبل وصول الكتاب إليه وقبل قراءته؛ لأنَّ العلم بالمقتضى به شرط لوجوب القضاء، فلم يكن النقلُ تامّاً، فيبطلُ بموتِ القاضي كما في سائر الأقضية إذا مات القاضي قبل تمامها. بخلاف شهودِ الفروع إذا مات الأصولُ بعد أدائهم الشهادة؛ لأنهم أوجبوا الحكم على القاضي بشهادتهم، فلا يسقط عنه الوجوب، أو بموت الفروع، كما في سائر الشهادات إذا مات الشهود بعد الأداء قبل الحكم بشهادتهم، فإنّه لا يسقط عنه الوجوب، فكذا هذا. كذا في «التبيين».
قوله: وعزله؛ وكذا بخروجه عن الأهلية؛ كالجنون والفسق؛ لأنَّ الخروج كالعزل، والإخراج حكماً لكونه واحداً من الرعايا، فكتابه لا يقبل كخطابه؛ لانتفاء الولاية الشرعية.
قوله: قبل وصوله؛ قال في «الفتح»: العبارة الجيدة أن يقال: لو مات قبل قراءة الكتاب لا قبل وصوله؛ لأنَّ وصوله قبل ثبوته عند المكتوب إليه وقراءته لا يوجب شيئا. انتهى. لو مات القاضي الكاتب بعدما قرأ الكتاب لا يبطل في ظاهر الرواية، ويحكم به المكتوب إليه؛ لأنه وجب عليه، والقضاء به بالقراءة، فلا يبطل بالموت، كما لو مات الشهاد بعد أداء الشهادة قبل الحكم بها. كما صرح به العلامةُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ، وغيره.
His statement: 'it becomes void ... etc.'; Abu Yusuf said: It does not become void, rather the addressee judge should judge by it, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration. This is in 'al-Ramz'. Their proof: The writing judge is like the performance of branch witnesses' testimony, because he conveys the testimony of those who witnessed the right to the addressee judge, and the conveyance has been completed by writing, so it becomes like branch witnesses when they die after performing testimony before judgment, for that does not prevent judgment, so here too, and likewise the ruling for every witness who dies after performing testimony before judgment. Our proof: Even though the writing judge conveys the testimony of those who witnessed in his presence, this conveyance has the effect of a judgment. Do you not see that this conveyance is only valid from a judge, its number is not required, nor the wording of testimony, and the writing judge is obligated to this conveyance upon hearing the evidence. Whatever is obligatory upon a judge by hearing evidence is by way of judgment. So it is established that this conveyance has the effect of a judgment but is not yet complete, because its completion requires judgment by the addressee, and judgment is not obligatory upon the addressee before the book reaches him and before its reading, because knowledge of what is required is a condition for the obligation of judgment, so the conveyance is not complete, so it becomes void by the death of the judge as in all other judgments when the judge dies before their completion. Unlike branch witnesses when the original witnesses die after their testimony, because they made judgment obligatory upon the judge by their testimony, so the obligation does not fall away, nor by the death of the branch witnesses, as in all testimonies when witnesses die after performance before judgment by their testimony, the obligation does not fall away. So here too. This is in 'al-Tabyin'. His statement: 'and his dismissal'; likewise by his loss of legal capacity, like insanity and transgression, because loss is like dismissal, and being taken out effectively because he becomes one of the subjects, so his book is not accepted like his speech, due to the absence of legal authority. His statement: 'before its arrival'; he said in 'al-Fath': the good expression is to say: if he dies before the reading of the book, not before its arrival, because its arrival before its establishment with the addressee and its reading does not necessitate anything. End. If the writing judge dies after the book has been read, it does not become void in the apparent narration, and the addressee judge judges by it, because it became obligatory upon him, and judgment by it is by reading, so it does not become void by death, as if the witnesses died after testimony before judgment. As stated by al-Zayla'i and others.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage deals with a key dispute in Hanafi fiqh: if a judge writes a judgment (kitab al-qadi) and then dies or is dismissed before the book is executed by the receiving judge, does the book become void? The base text states it becomes void (يبطل). Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the writing judge is like branch witnesses (shuhud al-far') who convey testimony; once the conveyance is written, it is complete, and death does not affect it. This view is also attributed to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad. The Hanafi response (لنا) provides the reasoning: the judge's conveyance is not mere testimony; it has the legal effect of a judgment (qada). This is evidenced by the facts that only a judge can make this conveyance, it does not require a specific number of witnesses or the wording of testimony, and it is obligatory upon the judge upon hearing evidence. Since it has the effect of a judgment, it is incomplete until the receiving judge reads and acts on it. Therefore, if the judge dies before that, the conveyance is null, just as any incomplete judgment becomes void upon the judge's death. In contrast, branch witnesses' testimony imposes an obligation on the judge that does not fall away with death. The passage also addresses dismissal ('azl) and loss of legal capacity (like insanity or transgression): these also void the book because the judge is no longer a valid authority. A subtle refinement from al-Fath clarifies that the relevant moment is death before the book is read by the receiving judge, not before its physical arrival. An exception is noted: if the judge dies after the book has been read in the receiving judge's court, then the book remains valid because the judgment has been established. This is the relied-upon position according to al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
قاضي كاتب (qadi katib): The judge who writes and transmits the written judgment (kitab) to another judge.
شهود الفرع (shuhud al-far'): Branch witnesses; witnesses who testify based on the testimony of original witnesses (usul).
نقل (naql): Conveyance; the transmission of testimony or judgment from one judge to another via written book.
قضاء (qada): Judgment; the act of adjudication by a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa), what happens if the writing judge dies after the book has been read by the receiving judge?
A) The book becomes void because the judge died.
B) The book remains valid because the judgment was established by reading.
C) The book becomes void and must be re-issued by a new judge.
D) The receiving judge must wait for a new judge to confirm.

Key Takeaway
A written judgment is void if the writing judge dies or is dismissed before it is read by the receiving judge, because it is an incomplete judgment — an exception exists if the book has already been read.

Validity of a Judge's Letter After His Death or Dismissal
Ruling with proof using qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a judge's written testimony after his death or dismissal and apply the qiyas to distinguish it from subsidiary witness testimony.

Core Idea
A judge's written testimony is an incomplete judgment; death before reading voids it, but death after reading makes it binding.

This passage refines the Hanafi position on the effect of a judge's death or dismissal on his written letter (al-kitab) to another judge. The key insight is that the letter has the status of a judgment (qada') in progress, not mere testimony. Therefore, its completion depends on the addressee's reading. We will unpack the proof, the distinction from witness testimony, and the precise condition of reading.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the writing judge's transmission (naql) of testimony to another judge. The Hanafi ruling is that if the writer dies or is dismissed before the addressee reads the letter, the letter becomes void. If death occurs after reading, it is valid.
The dismissal includes loss of qualification (insanity, sin) as removal from office.
2
Understand the proof: why it is like a judgment
The author argues that this transmission has the ruling of a judgment because only a judge can perform it, no number of witnesses or specific wording is required, and the writer is obligated to write upon hearing evidence.
This sets the foundational analogy: the letter is an incomplete judgment.
3
Apply the qiyas to incomplete judgments
Just as any judgment is void if the judge dies before completion (e.g., before pronouncing the verdict), the letter is void if the writer dies before the addressee reads it. Reading is the completion of the judgment.
The condition for obligation is knowledge of the grounds (al-muqtada bihi) by the addressee.
4
Distinguish from subsidiary witnesses
The author rebuts the objection that this is like witnesses dying after testifying. In that case, the obligation on the judge does not fall because the judge has already heard the testimony. Here, the writer judge is acting as a judge, not a witness, and the addressee has not yet completed the judgment.
The comparison (qiyas) with witnesses is rejected because the nature of the act differs.
5
Refine the temporal condition: reading not arrival
The passage clarifies that the relevant moment is when the letter is read, not when it arrives. Arrival alone does not create obligation.
This is a critical refinement from al-Fath.

ولنا : إنَّ القاضي الكاتب وإن كان ينقل شهادة الذين شهدوا عنده إلا أنَّ لهذا النقل حكم القضاء، ألا ترى أنَّ هذا النقل لا يصح إلا من القاضي، ولا يشترط فيه عدده ، ولا لفظ الشهادة ، ووجب على القاضي الكاتب هذا النقل بسماع البينة. وما يجب على القاضي بسماع البينة قضاءً فثبت أن لهذا النقل حكم القضاء ولم يتم بعد ؛ لأنَّ تمامه يوجب القضاء على المكتوب إليه، ولا يجب القضاء على المكتوب إليه قبل وصول الكتاب إليه وقبل قراءته ؛ لأنَّ العلم بالمقتضـى بـه شـرط لـوجـوب القضاء، فلم يكن النقلُ تامّاً، فيبطلُ بموتِ القاضي كما في سائر الأقضية إذا مات القاضي قبل تمامها. بخلاف شهودِ الفروع إذا مات الأصولُ بعد أدائهم الشهادة ؛ لأنهم أوجبوا الحكم على القاضي بشهادتهم، فلا يسقط عنه الوجوب، أو بموت الفروع ، كما في سائر الشهادات إذا مات الشهود بعد الأداء قبل الحكم بشهادتهم، فإنّه لا يسقط عنه الوجوب، فكذا هذا. كذا في «التبيين».

[٢] قوله : قبل وصوله ؛ قال في «الفتح» (۳) : العبارة الجيدة أن يقال : لو مات قبل قراءة الكتاب لا قبل وصوله ؛ لأنَّ وصوله قبل ثبوته عند المكتوب إليه وقراءته لا يوجب شيئا. انتهى.

Summary of al-Nihayah for Umdat al-Ri'ayah. And his dismissal before its arrival. [1] Our proof: Although the writing judge transmits the testimony of those who testified before him, yet this transmission has the ruling of a judgment. Do you not see that this transmission is only valid from the judge, and the number (of witnesses) is not required in it, nor the wording of testimony, and the writing judge is obligated to this transmission upon hearing the evidence. And whatever is obligatory upon the judge upon hearing evidence as a judgment, then it is established that this transmission has the ruling of a judgment, but it is not yet complete because its completion would necessitate judgment upon the addressee, and judgment upon the addressee is not obligatory before the book reaches him and before his reading it, because knowledge of the grounds (al-muqtada bihi) is a condition for the obligation of judgment. So the transmission is not complete, so it becomes void by the death of the judge, just as in all other judgments if the judge dies before their completion. Unlike the subsidiary witnesses if the principal witnesses die after performing their testimony, because they (the subsidiary witnesses) have made the judgment obligatory upon the judge by their testimony, so the obligation does not fall from him; or by the death of the subsidiary witnesses, as in all testimonies if the witnesses die after giving evidence before judgment upon their testimony, then the obligation does not fall from him. So this is similar. This is in al-Tabyin. [1] His statement: 'and his dismissal' – likewise by his loss of qualification (al-ahliyyah) like insanity and sin, because removal (from office) is like dismissal, and removal by law because he is one of the subjects, so his letter is not accepted just as his speech is not accepted due to lack of legal authority. [2] His statement: 'before its arrival' – In al-Fath it is said: The better expression is to say: if he dies before reading the book, not before its arrival, because its arrival before the addressee's confirmation and reading does not necessitate anything. End. If the writing judge dies after the book is read, it does not become void in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), and the addressee judges by it, because it has become obligatory upon him, and judgment by it occurs through reading, so it does not become void by death, just as if the witnesses died after giving testimony before judgment upon it. As explicitly stated by Allamah al-Zayla'i and others.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a supercommentary (hashiya) on Sharh al-Wiqayah, refining the discussion of a judge's written testimony (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi). The author first establishes that the judge's writing (naql) has the ruling of a judgment (qada'), not mere testimony. Proof: Only a judge can write such a letter; it does not require two witnesses or the specific wording of testimony; the writer is obligated to write upon hearing evidence. This shows it is a judgment in progress. However, it is incomplete because the addressee judge must read it for the judgment to be binding. Knowledge of the grounds (muqtada) is a condition for obligation. Therefore, if the writer judge dies before the addressee reads it, the writing becomes void, just as any judgment is void if the judge dies before its completion. The author then distinguishes this from the case of subsidiary witnesses (shuhud al-furu') where the principal witnesses die after testifying: in that case, the obligation does not fall from the judge. Similarly, if the writer judge dies after the addressee reads the letter, the judgment is binding and not void. The author then explains marginal notes: 'and his dismissal' includes removal by insanity or sin because the judge loses legal authority. Also, the correct phrase is 'before reading' not 'before arrival' because arrival alone does not create obligation. Finally, the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwayah) is that if the judge dies after reading, the letter stands, analogized to witnesses dying after giving evidence before judgment.

Key Terms
النقل (al-naql): The transmission of a judge's written testimony (kitab) to another judge.
القضاء (al-qada'): A binding legal judgment issued by a qualified judge.
شهود الفروع (shuhud al-furu'): Subsidiary witnesses who testify regarding the testimony of the principal witnesses (shuhud al-asl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The letter becomes void and the addressee cannot judge by it.
B) The letter remains valid and the addressee judges by it.
C) The letter is suspended until a third judge reviews it.
D) The letter becomes invalid only if the writer judge died of natural causes.

Key Takeaway
A judge's written testimony is an incomplete judgment; it is void if the writer dies before the addressee reads it, but valid if death occurs after reading.

Death of the Addressee Judge and the Condition of General Address
Ruling on death of addressee judge and condition of general address, with Shafi'i and intra-Hanafi disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge's letter after the addressee's death, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
A judge's letter to a specific judge becomes void on that judge's death unless a general phrase is included; later Hanafis prefer Abu Yusuf's view that a general address suffices from the start.

This lesson examines a procedural issue in judicial correspondence (qada): does a judge's letter addressed to a specific judge remain valid if that judge dies before it arrives? The Hanafi school distinguishes between a personal reliance that ends with death and a general reliance that survives. The Shafi'i school disagrees, and even within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Yusuf held a more flexible view that eventually became the relied-upon position. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how Hanafis balance textual conditions with practical utility.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on voidance upon death
Identify the default Hanafi ruling: a letter written to a specific judge becomes void if that judge dies before receiving it, unless the writer added a general phrase after the name ('and to every judge whom this reaches'). The writer relied on that specific judge's knowledge and trustworthiness.
The basis is the analogy with entrusted property (al-umanā' fi al-amwal).
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the letter does not become void even without the general phrase; the successor judge may act on it. His proof: the letter is analogous to a testimony (shahada), which is not voided by the original recipient's death.
The Shafi'i view treats the letter as a permanent document.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The Hanafi response distinguishes the letter from testimony: it is a command or judgment based on personal reliance. Just as a trustee's authority ends at his death, so does a judge's authority to execute the letter. If the writer includes the general phrase, he has relied on all judges equally, so the letter survives.
This differentiation uses qiyas with trustees.
4
Describe the intra-Hanafi difference (Abu Yusuf) and the later tarjih
Abu Yusuf holds that the writer may initially address the letter generally ('to every judge whom this reaches') without naming anyone. Many later scholars (mashayikh) and the author of al-Fath consider this view more appropriate (awjah), because informing the addressee is achieved by generality just as by specificity.
This view avoids the problem of voidance altogether.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi
Under the original Hanafi view, a letter to a specific judge becomes void on his death unless general phrase added. Under the Shafi'i view, it never voids. Under Abu Yusuf's view, a general address from the start is valid, so no voidance issue arises. The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view.
Thus, modern Hanafi practice allows general addressing to avoid complications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا بموت المكتوب إليه إلا إذا كتب بعد اسمِهِ : وإلى كلِّ مَن يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين
وعند أبي يوسف : لا يُشترط أن يكتب بعد اسمه إلى قاض معين، بل يكفي أن يكتب ابتداءً : إلى كلِّ مَن يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين ؛ لأنَّ تعيين المكتوب إليه تضييق لا فائدة فيه
[١]قوله : وكذا ; يبطلُ الكتاب بموت القاضي المكتوب إليه إلا أن يقبل الكتاب إذا كتب من بعد اسمه : أي إلى فلان القاضي وإلى من يصل الكتاب إلـيـه مـن قضاة المسلمين ; لأنَّ الكاتب ما خصه فقد اعتمد عدالته وأمانته، والقضاة متفاوتون في ذلك، فصح التعيين.
وقال الشافعي : لا يبطل، وإن لم يقل ذلك، ويحكم القاضي الذي جاء بعده به ، كما لو قال : وإلى كلِّ مَن يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين.
ولنا : أنَّ القاضي الكاتب اعتمد على علم الأوَّلِ وأمانته، والقضاة يتفاوتون في الأمانة ، فصار نظير الأمناء في الأموال، بخلاف ما إذا قال : وإلى كلِّ مَن يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين ، فإنه اعتمد الكل، فكان مكتوباً إليهم.
بخلاف ما إذا قال ابتداء : إلى كلِّ مَن يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين، حيث لا يجوز أن يحكم به أحد ; لأنَّ إعلامَ ما في الكتاب والمكتوب إليه شرط ، وتمام الإعلام لا يحصل بهذا القدر، وإذا عين واحداً حصل التعريف، وصح كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي، وصار غيره تبعاً له.
وأجاز أبو يوسف له من غير تعيين أحدٍ من القضاة حين ابتلي بالقضاء، واستحسنه كثير من المشايخ وفي «الفتح» ) : وهو الأوجه ; لأن إعلام المكتوب إليه وإن كان شرطاً فبالعموم يعلمُ كما يعلم بالخصوص ، وليس العموم من قبل الإجمال والتجهيل، فصار قصديته وتبعيته سواء. كذا في «النهر»، وغيره.
And likewise by the death of the addressee, unless he wrote after his name: 'And to every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches.' And according to Abu Yusuf: It is not required that he write after his name to a specific judge; rather it is sufficient that he write initially: 'To every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches,' because specifying the addressee is a restriction with no benefit. [1] His statement: 'And likewise'; the letter becomes void by the death of the judge to whom it is written, unless the letter is accepted if he wrote after his name: i.e., 'To so-and-so the judge and to whomever this book reaches from the judges of the Muslims,' because the writer has not specified him specifically; he has relied on his justice and trustworthiness, and judges differ in that, so specification is valid. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It does not become void, even if he did not say that, and the judge who comes after him judges by it, just as if he said: 'And to every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches.' Our proof: The writing judge relied on the knowledge and trustworthiness of the first (addressee), and judges vary in trustworthiness, so it becomes analogous to trustees of wealth. Unlike when he says: 'And to every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches,' for then he relied on all, so it is written to them. Unlike when he says initially: 'To every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches,' at that time no one may judge by it, because informing (i'lam) of what is in the book and the addressee is a condition, and complete informing is not achieved by this amount. And if he specifies one, the identification is achieved, and the letter of judge to judge is valid, and others become followers of him. Abu Yusuf permitted for him without specifying any of the judges when he is afflicted with judgment (i.e., becomes a judge), and many masters (mashayikh) considered this good. In al-Fath: 'And it is the most appropriate (awjah), because informing the addressee, even if it is a condition, is achieved by generality just as by specificity, and generality is not from the perspective of vague and ignorance, so his being the intended addressee and his being a follower are equal.' This is in al-Nahr and others.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the judge's letter, now focusing on the death of the addressee judge. The Hanafi position: If the addressee dies before the letter reaches him, the letter becomes void unless the writer included a general phrase after the specific name: 'and to every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches.' The reason is that the writer relied on the specific judge's knowledge and trustworthiness; if that judge dies, the basis of reliance is lost. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the letter remains valid for any successor judge, just as if it were addressed generally. The Hanafi response: relying on one judge's specific attributes makes it analogous to entrusting wealth to a specific trustee; if that trustee dies, the trust does not transfer automatically. However, if the writer includes the general phrase, he has relied on all judges equally. There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the writer may initially write 'to every judge' without specifying anyone, and this is valid for the first addressee who receives it. Many later scholars (mashayikh) and the author of al-Fath consider this opinion more appropriate (awjah), because informing the addressee is achieved by the general wording just as by a specific name, and generality does not imply ambiguity. Thus, according to Abu Yusuf and the preferred view, a general address is sufficient.

Key Terms
المكتوب إليه (al-maktub ilayh): The addressee judge to whom the letter is written.
إلى كل من يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين (ila kull man yasil ilayhi min qudat al-muslimin): The general address phrase 'to every judge among the Muslims whom this reaches' that renders the letter valid for any recipient.
أوجه (awjah): The most appropriate or stronger opinion from later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because the addressee must be named.
B) It is valid only if the writer knows all judges personally.
C) It is valid and the first judge who receives it may act on it.
D) It is valid only if two witnesses are present.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter to a specific judge becomes void upon that judge's death unless a general phrase is included; the mufta bihi allows a general address from the start, avoiding the voidance issue.

Validity of a Judge's Letter After the Recipient's Death
Dispute on validity of judge's letter after recipient judge dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on the validity of a judge's letter after the recipient judge dies, and evaluate the legal reasoning and preferred view.

Core Idea
A judge's letter dies with its recipient unless general wording is added — later Hanafis allowed general addressing.

This passage discusses an important procedural issue in judicial administration: what happens to a judge's written communication when the judge to whom it was written passes away? The Hanafi school has an internal ikhtilaf, and there is also a cross-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the conditions for the validity of judicial letters and the role of intention and specificity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa
Identify that according to the matn (and Abu Hanifa), the letter becomes void upon the death of the addressee unless the writer added a general phrase after the name, e.g., 'and to every judge who receives it among the Muslim judges.'
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Note that Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that general addressing suffices from the start; specification is unnecessary. Also note al-Shafi'i's view that the letter remains valid even without the general phrase.
The opposing schools are both within and outside the Hanafi school.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the reasoning: Abu Yusuf argues specification is a restriction with no benefit; al-Shafi'i analogizes to when the general phrase is used.
Pay attention to the basis of their arguments.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi counter-argument (lana): the writing judge relied on the specific trustworthiness and knowledge of the named judge, analogous to trustees over property. General wording would indicate reliance on all, which is different.
This is an istihsan based reasoning.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, if the recipient dies, the letter is void unless general phrase; under Abu Yusuf, it remains valid if written generally. Later scholars (mashayikh, al-Fatḥ, al-Nahr) preferred Abu Yusuf's view, making general addressing sufficient.
The mufta bihi now is that the letter does not become void if general wording is used, even without specifying a name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[كِتَابُ الْقَضَاءِ الْحَبْسِ وَكِتَابُ الْقَاضِي إِلَى الْقَاضِي]

[(وَكَذَا بِمَوْتِ الْمَكْتُوبِ إِلَيْهِ إِلَّا إِذَا كَتَبَ بَعْدَ اسْمِهِ: وَإِلَى كُلِّ مَنْ يَصِلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ)]
وَعِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ: لَا يُشْتَرَطُ أَنْ يَكْتُبَ بَعْدَ اسْمِهِ إِلَى قَاضٍ مُعَيَّنٍ، بَلْ يَكْفِي أَنْ يَكْتُبَ ابْتِدَاءً: إِلَى كُلِّ مَنْ يَصِلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ؛ لِأَنَّ تَعْيِينَ الْمَكْتُوبِ إِلَيْهِ تَضْيِيقٌ لَا فَائِدَةَ فِيهِ.

[١] قَوْلُهُ: وَكَذَا؛ يَبْطُلُ الْكِتَابُ بِمَوْتِ الْقَاضِي الْمَكْتُوبِ إِلَيْهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقْبَلَ الْكِتَابُ إِذَا كَتَبَ مِنْ بَعْدِ اسْمِهِ: أَيْ إِلَى فُلَانٍ الْقَاضِي وَإِلَى مَنْ يَصِلُ الْكِتَابُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ؛ لِأَنَّ الْكَاتِبَ مَا خَصَّهُ فَقَدِ اعْتَمَدَ عَدَالَتَهُ وَأَمَانَتَهُ، وَالْقُضَاةُ مُتَفَاوِتُونَ فِي ذَلِكَ، فَصَحَّ التَّعْيِينُ.
وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ: لَا يَبْطُلُ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَقُلْ ذَلِكَ، وَيَحْكُمُ الْقَاضِي الَّذِي جَاءَ بَعْدَهُ بِهِ، كَمَا لَوْ قَالَ: وَإِلَى كُلِّ مَنْ يَصِلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ.
وَلَنَا: أَنَّ الْقَاضِيَ الْكَاتِبَ اعْتَمَدَ عَلَى عِلْمِ الْأَوَّلِ وَأَمَانَتِهِ، وَالْقُضَاةُ يَتَفَاوَتُونَ فِي الْأَمَانَةِ، فَصَارَ نَظِيرَ الْأُمَنَاءِ فِي الْأَمْوَالِ، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ: وَإِلَى كُلِّ مَنْ يَصِلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ، فَإِنَّهُ اعْتَمَدَ الْكُلَّ، فَكَانَ مَكْتُوبًا إِلَيْهِمْ.
بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا قَالَ ابْتِدَاءً: إِلَى كُلِّ مَنْ يَصِلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ قُضَاةِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ، حَيْثُ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَحْكُمَ بِهِ أَحَدٌ؛ لِأَنَّ إِعْلَامَ مَا فِي الْكِتَابِ وَالْمَكْتُوبَ إِلَيْهِ شَرْطٌ، وَتَمَامُ الْإِعْلَامِ لَا يَحْصُلُ بِهَذَا الْقَدْرِ، وَإِذَا عَيَّنَ وَاحِدًا حَصَلَ التَّعْرِيفُ، وَصَحَّ كِتَابُ الْقَاضِي إِلَى الْقَاضِي، وَصَارَ غَيْرُهُ تَبَعًا لَهُ.
وَأَجَازَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ لَهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَعْيِينِ أَحَدٍ مِنَ الْقُضَاةِ حِينَ ابْتُلِيَ بِالْقَضَاءِ، وَاسْتَحْسَنَهُ كَثِيرٌ مِنَ الْمَشَايِخِ. وَفِي «الْفَتْحِ»: وَهُوَ الْأَوْجَهُ؛ لِأَنَّ إِعْلَامَ الْمَكْتُوبِ إِلَيْهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ شَرْطًا فَبِالْعُمُومِ يُعْلَمُ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ بِالْخُصُوصِ، وَلَيْسَ الْعُمُومُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الْإِجْمَالِ وَالتَّجْهِيلِ، فَصَارَ قَصْدِيَّتُهُ وَتَبِعِيَّتُهُ سَوَاءً. كَذَا فِي «النَّهْرِ» وَغَيْرِهِ.
[Book of Judgment, Imprisonment, and the Letter of a Judge to a Judge]

[(And similarly, it becomes void with the death of the person to whom it was written, unless he wrote after his name: 'and to every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges.')]
According to Abu Yusuf: It is not required that he write after his name to a specific judge; rather, it suffices that he write initially: 'To every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges,' because specifying the addressee is a restriction with no benefit.

[1] His statement: 'And similarly' – the letter becomes void with the death of the judge to whom it was written, unless the letter is accepted if he wrote after his name: i.e., to so-and-so the judge and to whoever receives the letter from among the Muslim judges; because the writer, by specifying him, relied on his uprightness and trustworthiness, and judges vary in that, so the specification is valid.
Al-Shafi'i said: It does not become void, even if he did not say that, and the judge who comes after him may rule by it, just as if he said: 'And to every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges.'
Our argument: The writing judge relied on the knowledge and trustworthiness of the first, and judges vary in trustworthiness, so it becomes analogous to trustees over property, unlike when he says: 'And to every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges,' for he relied on all, so they are all addressees.
Unlike when he says initially: 'To every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges,' where no one may rule by it; because informing of the contents of the letter and the addressee is a condition, and complete information is not achieved with this amount. And when he specifies one, identification is achieved, and the letter of a judge to a judge is valid, and others become subsidiary to him.
Abu Yusuf permitted it for him without specifying any of the judges when he was afflicted with judging, and many of the mashāyikh considered it istihsān. In al-Fatḥ: It is the more appropriate view, because informing the addressee, even if it is a condition, is achieved through generality just as it is achieved through specificity, and generality is not from the realm of ambiguity and ignorance. So being his intended recipient and being a subsidiary are the same. Thus in al-Nahr and others.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the validity of a judge's letter (kitāb al-qāḍī ilā al-qāḍī) when the recipient judge dies. The base text states that the letter becomes void upon the death of the addressee judge, unless the writer added the phrase 'and to every judge who receives it from among the Muslim judges' after the name of the specific judge. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that it is not necessary to specify a particular judge; rather, writing generally 'to every judge' is sufficient because specification is a useless restriction. The commentator explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the writing judge relied on the specific knowledge and trustworthiness of the named judge, and since judges differ in these qualities, the specification is meaningful. Al-Shafi'i holds that the letter does not become void even without the general phrase, and the successor judge may rule by it. The Hanafi response (lana) is that the writing judge relied on the first judge's qualities, analogous to trustees over property, so the letter dies with him unless general wording was used. However, if the writer initially wrote generally without specifying anyone, that is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because the condition of informing the specific addressee is not met. Abu Yusuf, when pressed by the need for judges, allowed general addressing, and many later scholars (mashāyikh) considered this istihsān. The text from al-Fatḥ and al-Nahr supports Abu Yusuf's view on the basis that general information is as effective as specific. The relied-upon position (mufṭā bihī) among later Hanafis is Abu Yusuf's view, as the passage indicates.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitāb al-qāḍī ilā al-qāḍī): A written judgment or communication sent by one judge to another judge, documenting a ruling or evidence.
المكتوب إليه (al-maktūb ilayhi): The judge to whom the letter is addressed.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning in Hanafi fiqh that allows departure from qiyas for a stronger evidence or necessity.
فتاوى المتأخرين (fatāwā al-muta'akhkhirīn): Fatwas of the later scholars, which often have precedence over earlier views in practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The letter becomes void because the writer relied on that judge's specific knowledge and trustworthiness.
B) The letter remains valid because the requirement of informing the addressee is a condition that can be satisfied by general address.
C) The letter becomes void unless the writer added 'and to every judge' after the name.
D) The letter remains valid only if the writer initially addressed all judges generally.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a judge's letter after the recipient's death depends on whether general wording was used; later Hanafi scholars adopted Abu Yusuf's view that general addressing suffices, making the letter valid.

Effect of Death of the Litigant on the Judge's Letter
Ruling that judge's letter is valid against the litigant's successor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge's letter is not invalidated by the death of a litigant and identify its application to heirs and executors.

Core Idea
Death does not invalidate a judge's letter; it binds the heir who stands in the litigant's place.

When a litigant dies during a lawsuit, does the case collapse? In Hanafi law, the death of a party does not nullify the judge's writ. Instead, the ruling is transferred to the legal successor—the heir or executor. This lesson examines the rationale and scope of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that the judge's letter is executed (yanfudh) against the heir of the deceased litigant. This means the letter remains legally valid and enforceable.
2
Understand the rationale
The heir stands in the place of the deceased (qāma maqāmahu). In Hanafi law, the rights and obligations of a person, including the effects of a pending lawsuit, transfer to the legal successor upon death.
3
Note the scope
The ruling applies regardless of whether the litigant was the plaintiff or defendant, and regardless of whether the letter was dated before or after the death. It also applies to the executor (waṣī) of the deceased.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
Lawsuits (khuṣūmāt) do not automatically extinguish with death; they transmit to heirs. This ensures continuity of judicial processes and prevents delay or evasion of judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَإِنْ مَاتَ الْخَصْمُ يَنْفُذُ عَلَى وَارِثِهِ)

[أي لا يبطل الكتاب بموت الخصم، بل ينفذ على وارثه؛ لأنه قائم مقامه، وإطلاق العبارة يشمل المدعي والمدعى عليه، ويشمل ما إذا كان تاريخ الكتاب بعد موت المطلوب أو قبله، وكذا ينفذ على وصيه.]
(And if the litigant dies, it is executed against his heir.)

[i.e., the letter is not invalidated by the death of the litigant; rather, it is executed against his heir because he (the heir) stands in his place. The general wording includes both the plaintiff and the defendant, and includes whether the date of the letter is after the death of the respondent or before it. Similarly, it is executed against his executor.]
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage states a straightforward ruling: The letter (kitāb al-qāḍī) is not invalidated by the death of a litigant (khaṣm). Instead, it is enforceable against the litigant's heir (wārith) because the heir stands in the place of the deceased. This applies whether the litigant is the plaintiff (mudda'ī) or the defendant (mudda'ā 'alayh). It also applies regardless of whether the letter was written before or after the death of the person claimed against (maṭlūb). Furthermore, it is also enforceable against the deceased's executor (waṣī). The reasoning is that the heir succeeds to the rights and obligations of the deceased, including the effect of the judge's letter. This ruling is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that lawsuits (khuṣūmāt) are not automatically extinguished by death but transfer to the legal successors.

Key Terms
الخصم (al-khaṣm): A litigant, either the plaintiff or the defendant in a legal case.
ينفذ على وارثه (yanfudh 'alā wārithihi): The ruling is executed and binding upon the heir.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed in the deceased's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The letter becomes void and must be reissued for the heirs.
B) The letter is executed against the heir or executor.
C) The letter is only valid if the deceased was the plaintiff.
D) The letter becomes void if it was written after the death of the defendant.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter remains valid after the litigant's death and is binding on the heir or executor.

Execution of Judge's Document Against Heir Upon Litigant's Death
Ruling on enforceability of judge's document after litigant's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding the enforceability of a judge's document upon the death of a litigant and identify against whom it is executed.

Core Idea
A litigant's death does not invalidate the judge's document; it is executed against the heir.

In judicial proceedings, a judge may issue a written document (kitab) recording a judgment or evidence. A common question arises: does this document become void if the litigant dies? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that the document remains effective and passes to the litigant's heir, who stands in his place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the core ruling: the judge's document is executed against the heir upon the litigant's death.
The text says 'yenfad' meaning it is enforced.
2
Understand the basis
Explain the rationale: the heir stands in the place of the deceased litigant (qā'im maqāmahu).
This is a principle of substitution in legal obligations.
3
Determine the scope
Recognize that the ruling applies to both plaintiff and defendant, regardless of whether the document was dated before or after the death.
The general wording covers all cases.
4
Extend to the executor
Note that the ruling also applies to the executor (wasi) of the deceased.
The executor manages the estate, so the document is executed against him.

وإن مات الخصمُ ينفد على وارثِه (۱) قوله : ينفذ على وارثه ؛ أي لا يبطل الكتاب بموت الخصم ، بل ينفذ على وارثه ؛ لأنه قائم مقامه، وإطلاق العبارة يشمل المدعي والمدعى عليه، ويشمل ما إذا كان تاريخ الكتاب بعد موت المطلوب أو قبله، وكذا ينفذ على وصيه.

(1) His statement: 'is executed against his heir' means that the document is not invalidated by the death of the litigant, but rather is executed against his heir because he stands in his place. The general wording includes the plaintiff and the defendant, and includes whether the date of the document is after the death of the requested party or before it. Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the judge's written document (kitab) when a litigant dies. The commentator explains that the death of the litigant does not nullify the document; rather, it is enforceable against the litigant's heir, who stands in his place. The ruling applies whether the document's date is before or after the death of the person against whom the claim was made. It also applies to the executor (wasi) if the deceased left one.

Key Terms

الوارث (al-wārith): The legal heir of the deceased litigant.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed by the deceased to manage his affairs.

Validity of Women's Judgment and Delegation of Judicial Authority
Rulings on women judges and delegation of judicial authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on women's judicial capacity and explain the delegation rules for judges, distinguishing between agency and judicial deputyship.

Core Idea
A woman's judgment is valid in financial matters but not in hudud and qisas; a delegate judge's deputy is not removed by the delegate's dismissal or death.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya discusses two important topics: the capacity of women to serve as judges and the rules governing judicial delegation. The Hanafi school holds that women are qualified to judge in matters where their testimony is accepted, but not in hudud and qisas. The text also clarifies that when a judge delegates authority, the deputy becomes the deputy of the original appointing authority, not the delegate, and thus is not removed by the delegate's dismissal or death. Understanding these distinctions is essential for proper application of Islamic judicial principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on women's judgment
Locate the phrase 'Wa saḥḥa qaḍā' al-mar'ah illā fī ḥadd wa qīṣāṣ' and note that the ruling states her judgment is valid except in hudud and qisas. Ask yourself: What is the basis for this exception?
The exception is linked to her testimony not being accepted in these matters.
2
Analyze the supporting evidence
Examine the qiyas: women are like men in being qualified to testify in financial matters, so they are qualified to judge. Also consider the rebuttal of the hadith 'No people will succeed...' which is interpreted as a deficiency in the situation, not a prohibition.
The dalil is both qiyas and a reinterpretation of the hadith.
3
Understand the delegation rules
Focus on the phrase 'lā yastakhlif qāḍī...'. The passage distinguishes between a judge who has been given general delegated authority (mufawwaḍ) and a limited delegate. The deputy of a mufawwaḍ is not removed by his dismissal or death because he is actually the deputy of the original (al-aṣīl).
This contrasts with agency (wakālah), where the agent is removed by the principal's death.
4
Apply the distinction to practical scenarios
Consider a scenario: The ruler appoints a judge (mufawwaḍ), who then appoints a deputy to hear cases. The ruler later dismisses the judge. Does the deputy lose his authority? According to this text, he does not, because he is the deputy of the ruler (the original), not of the judge.
This ensures continuity in judicial proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل (وصح قضاء المرأةِ إلا في حد وقود) ؛ لأنَّ شهادتها لا تُقْبَلُ فيهما ، (ولا يستخلف قاض، ولا يوكَّلُ وكيل إلا مَن فُوِّض إليه ذلك، ففي المفوض نائبه لا ينعزل بعزله وبموته موكلاً ، بل هو نائب الأصيل)، إنما قال موكلاً ؛ لأنَّ في الوكالة يَنْعزِلُ الوكيل بموتِ موكله ، فأرادَ أن يُصَرِّح أن الوكيل هاهنا لا ينعزل بموت موكله ؛ لأنه في الحقيقة ليس نائبه ، بل هو نائب الأصيل، وأما في القضاء فإِنَّ النَّائب لا يَنْعزِلُ بموتِ المنوب عنه، فخص الموكل بالذكرِ ؛ لأن الاشتباه فيه ولا شُبهة في باب القضاء، فلم يذكر، ثم قال : بل هو نائب الأصيل ؛ ففي التوكيل ينعزل بموت الأصيل، وفي القضاء لا ينعزل. [۱] قوله : وصح قضاء المرأة، وعن بعض الأئمّة : لا يجوز أن تتولّى المرأة ؛ لقصور عقلها، قلنا : هي من أهل الشهادة كالرجل ، قال العتابي » في «شرح الجامع الكبير»: امرأة قلّدت القضاء قضت في أموال صح؛ لأنها تصلح شاهدة في باب المال ، فتصلح قاضية. انتهى. وقوله : «لا يفلح قوم ولوا أمرهم امرأة ) ، يدل على نقصان ذلك الحال، لا (1) على عدم جواز توليتها. (٢) قوله : إلا في حد وقود ؛ فإن قضاءها فيهما لا يقبل، ولو قضت بالحدودِ والقصاص، وأمضاه قاض آخر يرى جوازه نفذ بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ نفس القضاء مجتهد فيه، فإنَّ شريكاً كان يجوزُ شهادة النساء مع رجل في الحدود والقصاص. كذا في «الرمز»). (۳) قوله : ولا يستخلف قاض ؛ قاضياً ؛ لأنه قُلدَ القضاء دون أن يُقلّده لغيره
Chapter: (And the judgment of a woman is valid except in hudud and qisas) because her testimony is not accepted in these two matters. (And a judge may not appoint a deputy, nor may he appoint an agent except for one who has been delegated that authority. So in the case of a general delegate, his deputy is not removed by his [the delegate's] dismissal or by the death of his principal; rather, he is the deputy of the original [appointing authority].) He only said 'principal' because in agency the agent is removed by the death of his principal, so he wanted to clarify that the agent here is not removed by the death of his principal because in reality he is not his deputy but the deputy of the original. As for the judiciary, the deputy is not removed by the death of the one who appointed him, so he specified the principal because the confusion lies there, and there is no ambiguity in the matter of judgment, so he did not mention it. Then he said: 'Rather, he is the deputy of the original.' So in agency, he is removed by the death of the original, and in judgment, he is not removed. [1] His statement: 'And the judgment of a woman is valid' — and some imams say: it is not permissible for a woman to assume judgment due to deficiency of her intellect. We say: she is among those whose testimony is accepted like a man. Al-‘Attabi said in Sharh al-Jami‘ al-Kabir: A woman who is appointed as a judge and rules in financial matters is valid because she is suitable as a witness in financial matters, so she is suitable as a judge. End. And his statement: 'No people will succeed who entrust their affairs to a woman' indicates the deficiency of that situation, not the impermissibility of her appointment. (2) His statement: 'except in hudud and qisas' — for her judgment in these two is not accepted. And if she judges in hudud and qisas, and another judge who considers it permissible ratifies it, it is executed by consensus because the judgment itself is subject to ijtihad. Indeed, Sharik used to permit the testimony of women with a man in hudud and qisas. Thus in al-Ramz. (3) His statement: 'And a judge may not appoint a deputy' — because he was appointed as a judge without appointing someone else.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage discusses two main issues: the validity of a woman's judgment and the rules of judicial delegation. First, the ruling: a woman's judgment is valid except in hudud and qisas, because her testimony is not accepted in those matters. The commentator addresses the objection that women cannot be judges due to intellectual deficiency, citing that women are like men in being qualified to testify, hence qualified to judge in financial matters. The hadith 'No people will succeed...' is interpreted as a criticism of the situation, not a prohibition. Second, the passage explains that a judge cannot appoint a deputy or agent unless he has been specifically authorized to do so. In the case of a general delegate (mufawwad), his deputy is not removed by the delegate's dismissal or death, because the deputy is actually the deputy of the original appointing authority (the imam or the one who delegated). This distinction is clarified to avoid confusion with agency, where the agent is removed by the principal's death.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishments prescribed by Islamic law (e.g., for theft, adultery, false accusation).
قود (qiṣāṣ): Retributive justice, specifically the law of retaliation for murder or bodily harm.
مفوّض (mufawwaḍ): A judge who has been given general delegated authority (tafwid) by the ruler to act with full discretion in judicial matters.
نائب الأصيل (nā'ib al-aṣīl): The deputy of the original appointing authority (the ruler or the person who delegated), not of the immediate delegator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hudud and qisas
C) All civil cases
D) Only hudud, not qisas

Key Takeaway
A woman's judicial capacity is recognized in Hanafi fiqh for financial matters, and a deputy judge appointed by a general delegate remains in office even after the delegate's removal.

Validity of a Woman's Judgeship in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling and proof for woman's judgeship except hadd and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman's judgeship, identify the exceptions of hadd and qisas, and explain the proof from testimony and the refutation of the hadith objection.

Core Idea
A woman's judgment is valid in property matters but not in hadd and qisas, due to her testimony not being accepted there.

This passage from the Hanafi tradition addresses a contested issue: may a woman serve as a judge? The matn affirms her judgeship is valid except in fixed punishments (hadd) and retaliation (qisas). The commentary expands on the reasoning, responding to objections by citing her eligibility to testify and offering a nuanced interpretation of the prophetic hadith often used to oppose women's leadership. We will unpack the legal proof and the scope of her judicial authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the matn: 'The judgment of a woman is valid except in hadd and qisas.' This is the core ruling. Determine what is permitted and what is excluded.
The ruling applies to all other areas, especially property and financial matters.
2
Locate the primary dalil for the exception
The matn gives the reason: 'because her testimony is not accepted in them.' This links the exception to the rules of testimony. Find the basis for this in Hanafi law: a woman's testimony is accepted in property but not in hadd and qisas.
This is a talil (causal reasoning) based on qiyas with witness capacity.
3
Analyse the response to opposing views
The commentator mentions that some imams reject women's judgeship due to intellectual deficiency. The Hanafi response asserts she is 'from the people of testimony like a man.' Follow the citation of al-‘Attabi: if she can witness in property, she can judge in property.
This uses qiyas: the qualification for testimony implies qualification for judgment.
4
Examine the interpretation of the opposing hadith
The hadith 'No people succeed who entrust their affairs to a woman' is cited. The commentator argues it indicates deficiency of the situation (naqs al-hal), not prohibition of the act itself. Understand how the Hanafi school limits the hadith's applicability.
This is a key example of ta'wil (interpretive restriction) to preserve the ruling.
5
Consider the exceptional case of enforcement
If a woman judges in hadd or qisas and another judge who considers it permissible enforces it, it is executed by consensus (ijma). Why? Because the ruling itself is subject to ijtihad, as shown by Shuraih permitting women's testimony with a man in these cases.
This highlights the role of ijtihad in jurisdictional validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
وصح قضاء المرأةِ إلا في حد وقصاص؛ لأنَّ شهادتها لا تُقْبَلُ فيهما.
قوله: وصح قضاء المرأة، وعن بعض الأئمّة: لا يجوز أن تتولّى المرأة؛ لقصور عقلها، قلنا: هي من أهل الشهادة كالرجل، قال العتابي في «شرح الجامع الكبير»: امرأة قلّدت القضاء قضت في أموال صح؛ لأنها تصلح شاهدة في باب المال، فتصلح قاضية. انتهى. وقوله: «لا يفلح قوم ولوا أمرهم امرأة»، يدل على نقصان ذلك الحال، لا على عدم جواز توليتها. قوله: إلا في حد وقصاص؛ فإن قضاءها فيهما لا يقبل، ولو قضت بالحدود والقصاص، وأمضاه قاض آخر يرى جوازه نفذ بالإجماع؛ لأنَّ نفس القضاء مجتهد فيه، فإنَّ شريكاً كان يجوز شهادة النساء مع رجل في الحدود والقصاص. كذا في «الرمز».
Section: The judgment of a woman is valid except in [cases of] hadd and qisas, because her testimony is not accepted in them. He said: 'And the judgment of a woman is valid'; and from some imams: it is not permissible for a woman to undertake [judgeship] due to deficiency of her intellect. We said: She is one of the people of testimony like a man. Al-‘Attabi said in 'Sharh al-Jami‘ al-Kabir': A woman who is appointed to the judiciary judges regarding property validly, because she is fit to be a witness in property matters, so she is fit to be a judge. End. And his statement: 'No people succeed who entrust their affairs to a woman' indicates deficiency of that situation, not the impermissibility of her undertaking it. His statement: 'except in hadd and qisas'; so her judgment in them is not accepted, and if she judged concerning hudud and qisas, and another judge who considers it permissible enforces it, it is executed by consensus, because the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad; for indeed Shuraih used to permit the testimony of women with a man in hudud and qisas. Thus in 'al-Ramz'.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the validity of a woman's judgeship. The matn states that a woman's judgment is valid except in hadd (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliatory punishments), because her testimony is not accepted in those matters. The commentator expands: Some earlier imams argued that a woman cannot be a judge due to intellectual deficiency, but the Hanafi response is that she is qualified to testify like a man, and since she can be a witness in property matters, she can be a judge. The hadith 'No people succeed who entrust their affairs to a woman' indicates deficiency in the situation, not absolute prohibition. Regarding hadd and qisas, if a woman judges and another judge who deems her judgment valid enforces it, it is executed by consensus because the ruling itself is subject to ijtihad. The example of Shuraih is cited, who permitted women's testimony with a man in such cases.

Key Terms
قضاء المرأة (qada' al-mar'ah): The judgment or judicial authority of a woman; the Hanafi position holds it valid except in hadd and qisas.
حد وقصاص (hadd wa qisas): Hadd: fixed punishments (e.g., for theft, zina); qisas: retaliatory punishment for homicide/assault.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because all imams agreed on it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a woman's judgeship in all areas except hadd and qisas, based on her eligibility to testify in financial matters and a careful interpretation of the relevant hadith.

Delegation in Judiciary and Agency: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on judicial delegation, identify the exception for the mufawwad, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
An authorized judge's appointee is the deputy of the Imam, not the judge — thus not removed by the judge's removal.

This lesson examines the rules governing delegation in judiciary and agency. The matn establishes a general prohibition on delegation unless authorized, then distinguishes between the deputy's relationship to the original authority versus the intermediary. A key dispute with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools turns on whether the authorized intermediary (mufawwad) can remove his own appointee.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general rule: a judge cannot appoint a substitute, nor an agent appoint another agent, unless explicitly authorized. When authorized, the appointee is the deputy of the original authority (Imam or principal), not of the intermediary.
The matn explicitly says: 'بل هو نائب الأصيل' (rather he is the deputy of the original authority).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools argue that if the mufawwad judge removes his appointee, the appointee is removed, analogizing to agency where the principal can remove his agent.
The passage states: 'وقال الشافعي وأحمد: إذا عزل القاضي المفوض إليه نائبه ينعزل'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their dalil is qiyas: the mufawwad is like a principal, and a principal can remove his agent; therefore, the mufawwad can remove his appointee.
This analogy (qiyas) treats judicial delegation as a form of agency.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response rejects the analogy: in qada, the deputy is not the deputy of the mufawwad but of the Imam. The mufawwad is merely an intermediary (a conduit) for the original authority's appointment. Therefore, he does not have the power to remove.
Key distinction: 'نائبه لا ينعزل بعزله' (his deputy is not removed by his removal).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi fiqh, the deputy remains in office even if the mufawwad is removed or dies. Under Shafi'i/Hanbali, the deputy is removed with the mufawwad, potentially disrupting judicial continuity.
The text highlights the exception: only if the mufawwad is explicitly told 'استبدل من شئت' may he remove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
(ولا يستخلف قاض، ولا يوكَّلُ وكيل إلا مَن فُوِّض إليه ذلك، ففي المفوض نائبه لا ينعزل بعزله وبموته موكلاً، بل هو نائب الأصيل)، إنما قال موكلاً؛ لأنَّ في الوكالة يَنْعزِلُ الوكيل بموتِ موكله، فأرادَ أن يُصَرِّح أن الوكيل هاهنا لا ينعزل بموت موكله؛ لأنه في الحقيقة ليس نائبه، بل هو نائب الأصيل، وأما في القضاء فإِنَّ النَّائب لا يَنْعزِلُ بموتِ المنوب عنه، فخص الموكل بالذكرِ؛ لأن الاشتباه فيه ولا شُبهة في باب القضاء، فلم يذكر، ثم قال: بل هو نائب الأصيل؛ ففي التوكيل ينعزل بموت الأصيل، وفي القضاء لا ينعزل.
(قوله: ولا يستخلف قاض؛ قاضياً؛ لأنه قُلدَ القضاء دون أن يُقلّده لغيره، ولأنَّ الإمام إنما رضي بقضائه دون غيره، بخلاف الوصي حيث يملك الإيصاءَ إلى غيره ويملك التوكيل والعزل في حياته؛ لرضاء الموصي بذلك دلالة لعجزه. وبخلاف المستعير، فإنه له الإعارة بشرطها؛ لأنه لما ملك المنفعة ملك تمليكها، ذكره العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار، وبخلاف المأمور بالجمعة، حيث يجوز أن يستخلف؛ لكونها على شرف السقوط. كذا في «الرمز».)
(ولا يوكل وكيل وكيلاً؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل إنما رضي بتصرفِهِ دون غيره، إلا مَن فوَّضَ الإمام الموكَّلُ إليه ذلك الاستخلاف، بأن قال: ول أو وكل من شئت، فإنه يجوز حينئذ أن يستخلف القاضي قاضياً آخر، والموكل وكيلاً آخر.
وفيه أمر إلى أنه يستخلف دلالة، فلو جعل قاضي القضاة كان له الاستخلاف؛ لأن معناه المتصرف في القضاء تقليداً وعزلاً. وإلى أنَّ القاضي إذا أُذِنَ بالاستخلاف فاستخلف رجلاً، وأذن بالاستخلاف جاز له أن يستخلف ثُمَّ وثُمَّ. ذكره القُهُسْتاني ناقلا عن «الخلاصة».)
(إذا عرفت هذا قضى القاضي أو الوكيل المفوض إليه؛ أي الذي فوض إليه الاستخلافُ أو التوكيل نائبه؛ أي نائب القاضي، أو الوكيل لا ينعزل بعزله؛ أي بعزل المفوض إليه إياه؛ لأنَّه صار نائباً عن الأصيل، إلا إذا فوض إليه ذلك بأن قيل له من قبل السلطان أو الموكل: استبدل من شئت. فحينئذ يجوز له العزل، ولا يعزلُ الغائب بموته؛ أي المفوض إليه حال كونه موكلاً، بل هو؛ أي نائب المفوض إليه، نائب الأصيل، وهو السلطان أو الموكل إلا أنه في التوكيل ينعزل بموت الأصيل، وفي القضاء: لا ينعزل.
وقال الشافعي وأحمد: إذا عزل القاضي المفوض إليه نائبه ينعزل؛ لأنه كتوكيله، والموكل يملك عزل وكيله.
Section: A judge does not appoint a substitute, nor does an agent appoint an agent, except one who has been given authority for that. So in the case of the authorized [mufawwad], his deputy is not removed by his removal nor by the death of his principal, rather he is the deputy of the original authority. He only said 'principal' because in agency the agent is removed by the death of his principal; he intended to clarify that the agent here is not removed by the death of his principal, because in reality he is not his deputy, but the deputy of the original authority. As for in judiciary, the deputy is not removed by the death of the one who appointed him, so he specified the principal due to the confusion there, and there is no confusion in the matter of judiciary, so he did not mention it. Then he said: Rather he is the deputy of the original authority. So in agency he is removed by the death of the original authority, and in judiciary he is not removed.
(He said: A judge does not appoint a substitute judge, because he has been entrusted with the judiciary without entrusting it to another, and because the Imam only agreed to his judgment, not another's. Unlike the executor [wasi] who has the right to appoint another and to delegate and remove during his lifetime, due to the testator's implied consent because of his inability. And unlike the borrower [musta'ir] who may lend with its conditions, because when he gained ownership of usufruct he gained the right to transfer it, as mentioned by al-Tahtawi in his hashya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And unlike the one commanded to perform Friday prayer, who may appoint a substitute because it is on the verge of being dropped. Thus in al-Ramz.)
(An agent does not appoint another agent, because the principal only agreed to his action, not another's, except one whom the Imam has authorized to do so, by saying: 'Appoint or delegate whomever you wish.' In that case, the judge may appoint another judge, and the principal may appoint another agent. And in it is an indication that he may appoint by implication. So if one is made 'Judge of Judges' he has the right to appoint, because its meaning is 'the one who has authority over the judiciary in terms of appointment and removal.' And it indicates that if a judge is permitted to appoint, and he appoints a man and permits him to appoint, that man may then appoint, and so on. Al-Quhistani mentioned it, quoting from al-Khulasa.)
(If you know this, then a judge or an authorized agent — i.e., the one who has been authorized to appoint or delegate — his deputy is not removed by his removal, i.e., by the removal of the one who authorized him, because he has become a deputy of the original authority, unless he has been authorized in that, such as if it is said to him by the Sultan or the principal: 'Replace whomever you wish.' Then he may remove. And the absent one is not removed by his death, i.e., the authorized person while being a principal, rather he — i.e., the deputy of the authorized person — is the deputy of the original authority, which is the Sultan or the principal, except that in agency he is removed by the death of the original authority, and in judiciary he is not removed.
And al-Shafi‘i and Ahmad said: If the judge who has been authorized removes his deputy, he is removed, because it is like his agency, and the principal has the right to remove his agent.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on delegation of authority in judiciary and agency, accompanied by an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states that a judge cannot appoint a substitute (khalifah) and an agent (wakil) cannot appoint another agent unless specifically authorized. If a judge or agent is given authority to delegate (mufawwad), then his appointee is not removed by the removal or death of the original principal; rather, the appointee is the deputy of the original authority (the Imam or the principal). The commentator distinguishes between wakalah and qada: in wakalah, the agent is removed by the death of the principal; in qada, the deputy is not removed by the death of the one who appointed him. The exception is if the mufawwad is given explicit permission to replace, then he may do so. The commentator contrasts the judge with the executor (wasi), the borrower (musta'ir), and the one commanded to lead Friday prayer, who may delegate. Then the dispute: Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad argue that if the mufawwad judge removes his appointee, the appointee is removed, equating it to agency where the principal can remove his agent. The Hanafi position holds that the appointee is not removed because he is the deputy of the original authority, not of the mufawwad.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhlaaf): Appointing a substitute or deputy (khalifah) in judicial or agency matters.
مفوض (mufawwad): One who has been given authority by the Imam or principal to appoint a substitute or delegate.
نائب الأصيل (na'ib al-asil): Deputy of the original authority (the Imam or the principal); contrasts with the deputy of the intermediary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what happens to the appointee of a mufawwad judge when the mufawwad is removed?
A) The appointee is also removed
B) The appointee remains because he is the deputy of the Imam
C) The appointee becomes independent
D) The appointee must be reappointed by the new mufawwad

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the deputy of an authorized judge is the Imam's deputy, so he survives the judge's removal unless the Imam explicitly allows replacement.

The Deputy Judge’s Independence from the Appointer’s Dismissal
Debate on whether a deputy judge is dismissed when the intermediary is dismissed or dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the dismissal of a deputy judge and evaluate the Shafi'i/Hanbali counter-argument using the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A deputy judge is not dismissed when the appointing judge is dismissed; he is the sultan's deputy.

If a judge appointed by the sultan appoints a deputy, what happens to the deputy when the judge dies or is dismissed? This seemingly administrative question triggers a deep disagreement between the Hanafi school and the Shafi'i/Hanbali schools. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah surveys the debate and settles on the Hanafi view that the deputy remains in office because his authority derives directly from the sultan, not from the intermediary judge.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi rule: the deputy is not dismissed by the dismissal or death of the intermediary judge. The deputy is considered the deputy of the original authority (imam/sultan).
This applies to qada; in ordinary agency, the deputy is dismissed by the principal's death.
2
Identify the opposing school and their position
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that the deputy is dismissed when the intermediary is dismissed, because the relationship is analogous to agency (wakala).
Their analogy: the intermediary acts as an agent of the original authority, so he can dismiss his own deputy.
3
Follow the opposing proof
The opposing dalil: the intermediary's act of appointing a deputy is like appointing an agent; a principal (intermediary) can dismiss his agent (deputy).
This is a qiyas based on the similarity of contracts.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi rebuttal: the deputy is not the intermediary's agent but the sultan's direct appointee. The intermediary has no inherent power to dismiss unless explicitly authorized by the sultan.
The Hanafi argument rests on the principle that the appointment by the imam makes the deputy the imam's deputy, not the qadi's.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical consequence: under Hanafi, the deputy remains; under Shafi'i, he is dismissed. The relied-upon fatwa (al-Bazzazi) affirms the Hanafi view, and internal Hanafi disagreements (e.g., some say dismissal upon dismissal but not death) are overridden by the principle that the deputy is the sultan's deputy.
Al-Bazzazi's statement indicates the fatwa is on non-dismissal in both cases.

Source Text
