ولا يوكل وكيل وكيلاً ؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل إنما رضي بتصرفِهِ دون غيره، إلا مَن فوَّضَ الإمام الموكَّلُ إليه ذلك الاستخلاف، بأن قال : ول أو وكل من شئت، فإنه يجوز حينئذ أن يستخلف القاضي قاضياً آخر، والموكل وكيلاً آخر. وفيه أمر إلى أنه يستخلف دلالة، فلو جعل قاضي القضاة كان له الاستخلاف ؛ لأن معناه المتصرف في القضاء تقليداً وعزلاً. وإلى أنَّ القاضي إذا أُذِنَ بالاستخلاف فاستخلف رجلاً، وأذن بالاستخلاف جاز له أن يستخلف ثُمَّ وثُمَّ. ذكره القُهُسْتاني ناقلا عن («الخلاصة»). إذا عرفت هذا قضى القاضي أو الوكيل المفوض إليه ؛ أي الذي فوض إليه الاستخلافُ أو التوكيل نائبه ؛ أي نائب القاضي، أو الوكيل لا ينعزل بعزله ؛ أي بعزل المفوض إليه إياه ؛ لأنَّه صار نائباً عن الأصيل، إلا إذا فوض إليه ذلك بأن قيل له من قبل السلطان أو الموكل : استبدل من شئت. فحينئذ يجوز له العزل، ولا يعزلُ الغائب بموته ؛ أي المفوض إليه حال كونه موكلاً، بل هو ؛ أي نائب المفوض إليه، نائب الأصيل، وهو السلطان أو الموكل إلا أنه في التوكيل ينعزل بموت الأصيل، وفي القضاء : لا ينعزل. وقال الشافعي وأحمد له : إذا عزل القاضي المفوض إليه نائبه ينعزل ؛ لأنه كتوكيله، والموكل يملك عزل وكيله. ولنا : أنه لما صح الاستخلافُ من جهة الإمام كان نائباً عن الإمام، فلا يملك المفوِّضُ إليه عزله إلا أن يقول له الإمام : ول من شئت، واستبدل من شئت. ذكره العلامة الثمني ، وغيره. واعلم أنَّ هاهنا كلاماً طويلاً وأنا أذكره مختصراً ناقلاً عن «الأشباه والنظائر» قال فيه : قال في خزانة الفتاوى» : إذا مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، ولو مات واحد من الولاة انعزل خلفاؤه ، ولو مات الخليفة لا تنعزل ولاته وقضاته. انتهى. وفي «الخلاصة» وفي «هداية الناطقي»: لو مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، وكذا موتُ أمراء الناحية بخلاف موت الخليفة السلطانُ إذا عزل القاضي انعزل النائب ، بخلاف موت القاضي. وفي «المحيط»: إذا عزل السلطانُ القاضي انعزل نائبه، بخلاف ما إذا مات القاضي لا ينعزل نائبه هكذا قيل، وينبغي أن لا ينعزل النائب بعزل القاضي ؛ لأنه نائب السلطان، أو نائب العامة، ألا ترى أنه لا ينعزل بموت القاضي، وعليه كثير من المشايخ. انتهى. وفي «البزازية»: مات الخليفة وله أمراء وعمال، فالكل على ولايته، وفي «المحيط»: مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه ، وكذا أمراء الناحية بخلاف موت الخليفة ، وإذا عزل القاضي ينعزل نائبه ، وإذا مات لا والفتوى على أنه لا ينعزلُ بعزل القاضي ؛ لأنه نائب السلطان أو العامة، وبعزل نائب القاضي لا ينعزل القاضي. انتهى. وفي «العمادية» و«جامع الفصولين كما في «الخلاصة»، وفي فتاوى قاضي خان: وإذا مات الخليفة لا ينعزل قضاتُهُ وعماله ، وكذا لو كان القاضي مأذونـاً بالاستخلاف فاستخلف غيره ، ومات القاضي أو عزل لا ينعزل خليفته. انتهى. فتحرر من ذلك اختلاف المشايخ في انعزال النائب بعزل القاضي وموته، وقولُ البَزَّازِيُّ : الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزلُ بعزل القاضي، يدلُّ على أن الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بموته بالأولى، لكن علله بأنه نائب السلطان.
And a deputy may not appoint a deputy because the principal only consented to his own action, not to another's. Except for one to whom the imam or the principal delegated authority to appoint deputies, such as by saying: 'Appoint or deputize whomever you wish.' Then it is permissible for the judge to appoint another judge, and for the principal to appoint another agent. And it contains an implicit command to appoint a deputy. So if one is made Judge of Judges (Qadi al-Qudat), he may appoint deputies because that title implies authority over judicial appointments and dismissals. And if a judge is permitted to appoint deputies and he appoints a man, and that man is permitted to appoint deputies, then he may appoint successive deputies. This is mentioned by al-Quhustani, quoting from al-Khulasa. If you understand this, the judge or the delegated agent—meaning one who has been delegated the power of appointment—his deputy (i.e., the deputy judge or agent) is not dismissed by the dismissal of the one who delegated to him (i.e., the intermediary), because he has become a deputy of the original principal. Except if he was delegated authority to do so, such that it is said to him by the sultan or the principal: 'Replace whomever you wish.' Then it is permissible for him to dismiss, and the absent person is not dismissed by his death (i.e., the intermediary's death while being an agent). Rather, he (the deputy of the intermediary) is the deputy of the original principal, i.e., the sultan or the principal. However, in agency, the deputy is dismissed by the death of the original principal, but in judgeship, he is not dismissed. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: If the delegated judge dismisses his deputy, the deputy is dismissed because it is like his agency, and the principal has the power to dismiss his agent. Our proof: since the appointment of a deputy from the imam is valid, the deputy is the deputy of the imam, so the delegated person does not have the power to dismiss him unless the imam says to him: 'Appoint whomever you wish, and replace whomever you wish.' This is mentioned by al-Thamini and others. Know that there is a lengthy discussion here, which I will summarize, quoting from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir. He said: In Khizanat al-Fatawa: If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed. If one of the governors dies, his deputies are dismissed. If the caliph dies, his governors and judges are not dismissed. End. In al-Khulasa and Hidayat al-Natiqi: If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed, and likewise the death of district governors, unlike the death of the caliph. If the sultan dismisses the judge, the deputy is dismissed, unlike the death of the judge. In al-Muhit: If the sultan dismisses the judge, his deputy is dismissed, unlike when the judge dies—his deputy is not dismissed. Thus it is said, and it is preferable that the deputy is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge because he is the deputy of the sultan or of the public. Do you not see that he is not dismissed by the death of the judge? And many scholars hold this view. End. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the caliph dies and he has governors and officials, all remain in their office. In al-Muhit: If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed, and likewise district governors, unlike the death of the caliph. If the judge is dismissed, his deputy is dismissed; if he dies, no. And the fatwa is that he is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge because he is deputy of the sultan or the public, and by the dismissal of the deputy judge, the judge is not dismissed. End. In al-Amadiyya and Jami' al-Fusulayn, as in al-Khulasa, and in Fatawa Qadi Khan: If the caliph dies, his judges and officials are not dismissed. Similarly, if a judge is permitted to appoint deputies and he appoints someone, and the judge dies or is dismissed, his deputy is not dismissed. End. So it is established from this the difference among scholars regarding the dismissal of the deputy by the dismissal and death of the judge. Al-Bazzazi's statement that the fatwa is that he is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge indicates that he is not dismissed by his death a fortiori, but he justified it by saying he is the deputy of the sultan.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a detailed discussion of a dispute in qada and wakalah about whether a deputy appointed by an intermediary (e.g., a judge appointed by the sultan who then appoints a deputy judge) is dismissed when the intermediary is dismissed or dies. The passage begins by affirming the general rule that a deputy cannot further delegate unless specifically authorized. However, if the principal (imam/muwakkil) delegates full authority, successive appointments are allowed. The main issue arises when the intermediary is dismissed or dies: Is the deputy automatically dismissed? The Hanafi position, supported by many sources, is that the deputy is directly appointed by the original authority (imam/sultan or principal), not the intermediary, so dismissal of the intermediary does not affect the deputy. The Shafi'i and Hanbali view is that the deputy is like the intermediary's agent, so dismissal of the intermediary dismisses the deputy. The Hanafi argument is rational: since the appointment originated from the imam, the deputy is the imam's deputy; the intermediary has no power to dismiss unless explicitly authorized. The passage then surveys various Hanafi authorities (Khizanat al-Fatawa, al-Khulasa, al-Muhit, al-Bazzaziyya, etc.) showing internal differences on whether the deputy is dismissed upon the judge's death or dismissal. Some say the deputy is dismissed if the judge is dismissed but not if he dies; others say the opposite; the relied-upon fatwa (from al-Bazzazi) is that the deputy is not dismissed in either case because he is the deputy of the sultan or the public. This ruling applies specifically to judges (qadis) and governors, not to ordinary agents where the death of the principal does terminate the agency. The passage concludes by noting the difference of opinion but affirming that the fatwa favors non-dismissal.

Key Terms
نائب (na'ib): A deputy appointed by a judge or agent to act on behalf of the original authority.
مفوض إليه (mufawwad ilayh): The person to whom authority to appoint deputies or agents has been delegated (the intermediary).
استخلاف (istikhlaf): The act of appointing a deputy (khalifa) to act in one's place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi), what happens to a deputy judge when the judge who appointed him is dismissed?
A) The deputy judge is automatically dismissed.
B) The deputy judge remains in office because he is considered a deputy of the sultan, not of the dismissed judge.
C) The deputy judge is dismissed only if the sultan also dismisses him.
D) The deputy judge is dismissed only if the dismissal is in writing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a deputy judge's authority is direct from the sultan, so he is not dismissed when the appointing judge is removed.

Does a Deputy Judge Survive the Judge's Death? An Intra-Hanafi Analysis
Intra-Hanafi dispute on deputy's dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether a deputy judge is dismissed by the judge's death or removal, and explain which view is mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
The deputy judge is not dismissed by the judge's death—he is deputy of the ruler, not the judge.

This passage confronts a practical dilemma: when a judge dies or is removed, what happens to the deputies he appointed? The Hanafi school offers two conflicting answers. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how later scholars resolved the tension between judicial continuity and the principle of personal delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognise that the ruling is about the status of a deputy judge (khalīfah/nā'ib) after the death or dismissal (ʿazl) of the judge who appointed him.
The issue is not about the judge's own authority but about the deputy's continued validity.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Position A: The deputy is dismissed by the judge's death/dismissal. Position B (mufta bihi): The deputy is not dismissed because he is ultimately the deputy of the ruler or the public.
Both positions are found within the Hanafi school; the disagreement is intra-madhhab.
3
Trace the evidence for each position
For Position A: earlier sources like Khizānat al-Fatāwā and al-Khulāṣa state the deputy is dismissed. For Position B: later authorities like al-Muḥīṭ and al-Bazzāziyyah argue the deputy is the sultan's deputy, not the judge's personal appointee.
Note the shift over time: earlier views stressed personal delegation, later views emphasised public interest.
4
Examine the tarjīḥ (preference)
Al-Bazzāzī declares the fatwā is that the deputy is not dismissed by the judge's dismissal, and a fortiori not by his death. This relies on the analogy of the caliph: the caliph's death does not dismiss judges and governors.
The tarjīḥ is based on the argument that the deputy's authority stems from the ruler, not the judge.
5
Derive the thamarah (practical consequence)
Under the mufta bihi view, judicial proceedings conducted by the deputy after the judge's death remain valid. Under the other view, they would be void.
This prevents disruption in the judiciary, especially in large jurisdictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا : أنه لما صح الاستخلافُ من جهة الإمام كان نائباً عن الإمام، فلا يملك المفوِّضُ إليه عزله إلا أن يقول له الإمام : ول من شئت، واستبدل من شئت. ذكره العلامة الثمني ، وغيره. واعلم أنَّ هاهنا كلاماً طويلاً وأنا أذكره مختصراً ناقلاً عن «الأشباه والنظائر»(۱) قال فيه : قال في خزانة الفتاوى» : إذا مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، ولو مات واحد من الولاة انعزل خلفاؤه ، ولو مات الخليفة لا تنعزل ولاته وقضاته. انتهى. وفي «الخلاصة» وفي «هداية الناطقي»: لو مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه، وكذا موتُ أمراء الناحية بخلاف موت الخليفة السلطانُ إذا عزل القاضي انعزل النائب ، بخلاف موت القاضي. وفي «المحيط»: إذا عزل السلطانُ القاضي انعزل نائبه، بخلاف ما إذا مات القاضي لا ينعزل ،نائبه هكذا قيل، وينبغي أن لا ينعزل النائب بعزل القاضي ؛ لأنه نائب السلطان، أو نائب العامة، ألا ترى أنه لا ينعزل بموت القاضي، وعليه كثير من المشايخ. انتهى. وفي «البزازية»: مات الخليفة وله أمراء وعمال، فالكل على ولايته، وفي «المحيط»: مات القاضي انعزل خلفاؤه ، وكذا أمراء الناحية بخلاف موت الخليفة ، وإذا عزل القاضي ينعزل نائبه ، وإذا مات لا والفتوى على أنه لا ينعزلُ بعزل القاضي ؛ لأنه نائب السلطان أو العامة، وبعزل نائب القاضي لا ينعزل القاضي. انتهى. وفي «العمادية» و«جامع الفصولين كما في «الخلاصة»، وفي فتاوى قاضي خان: وإذا مات الخليفة لا ينعزل قضاتُهُ وعماله ، وكذا لو كان القاضي مأذونـاً بالاستخلاف فاستخلف غيره ، ومات القاضي أو عزل لا ينعزل خليفته. انتهى. فتحرر من ذلك اختلاف المشايخ في انعزال النائب بعزل القاضي وموته، وقولُ البَزَّازِيُّ : الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزلُ بعزل القاضي، يدلُّ على أن الفتوى على أنه لا ينعزل بموته بالأولى، لكن علله بأنه نائب السلطان.
And our proof: When deputization from the ruler is valid, the deputy is the ruler's deputy, so the one who delegated to him cannot dismiss him unless the ruler says to him: appoint whomever you wish and replace whomever you wish. This was mentioned by al-Thamini and others. Know that there is lengthy discussion here, but I will present it briefly, quoting from 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir'. He said therein: In 'Khizanat al-Fatawa': If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed. If one of the governors dies, his deputies are dismissed. If the caliph dies, his governors and judges are not dismissed. End. In 'al-Khulasa' and 'Hidayat al-Natiqi': If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed; likewise the death of district governors, unlike the death of the caliph. If the sultan dismisses the judge, the deputy is dismissed, unlike the death of the judge. In 'al-Muhit': If the sultan dismisses the judge, his deputy is dismissed; unlike if the judge dies, his deputy is not dismissed. Thus it is said, and it should be that the deputy is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge because he is the deputy of the sultan or the general public. Do you not see that he is not dismissed by the death of the judge? And many scholars follow this. End. In 'al-Bazzaziyya': If the caliph dies and has governors and officials, all remain in their positions. In 'al-Muhit': If the judge dies, his deputies are dismissed, and likewise district governors, unlike the death of the caliph. If the judge is dismissed, his deputy is dismissed, but if he dies, the fatwa is that he is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge because he is the deputy of the sultan or the general public. By the dismissal of the judge's deputy, the judge is not dismissed. End. In 'al-Imadiyya' and 'Jami' al-Fusulayn' like in 'al-Khulasa', and in Fatawa Qadi Khan: If the caliph dies, his judges and officials are not dismissed. Likewise, if the judge was authorized to appoint a deputy and appointed someone else, then the judge dies or is dismissed, his deputy is not dismissed. End. From this, the disagreement of scholars regarding the dismissal of the deputy by the dismissal or death of the judge is settled. The statement of al-Bazzazi: the fatwa is that he is not dismissed by the dismissal of the judge, indicates that the fatwa is that he is not dismissed by his death a fortiori, but he gave the reason that he is the deputy of the sultan.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute compilation regarding the effect of a judge's death or removal on his appointed deputies (khalifah/nā'ib). The core issue: Is a deputy judge appointed by a judge considered the judge's deputy or the ruler's deputy? The author, quoting multiple sources, shows two main positions: (1) The deputy is dismissed by the judge's death or dismissal because he is the judge's delegate. (2) The deputy is not dismissed because he is actually the ruler's deputy (nā'ib al-sulṭān) or the public's deputy (nā'ib al-'āmmah), especially if the judge had full delegation (istikhilāf) from the ruler. The sources cited (Khizānah, Khulāṣah, Muḥīṭ, Bazzāziyyah, etc.) reflect a shift: earlier views often equated the deputy with the judge's agent, while later authorities (like al-Bazzāzī) ruled that the fatwā is that the deputy is not dismissed by the judge's dismissal, and a fortiori not by his death. The passage ends by noting that in contemporary times, deputies are considered the judge's deputies from every angle, so they are dismissed by the judge's death or dismissal.

Key Terms
خليفة (khalīfah): Deputy judge appointed by the original judge to hear cases.
نائب (nā'ib): Deputy, representative. Refers to the deputy of a judge, ruler, or public.
عزل (ʿazl): Dismissal from office. The act of removing a judge or other official from their position.
مفوّض (mufawwiḍ): The one who delegates authority to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in this passage, what happens to a deputy judge when the judge who appointed him dies?
A) The deputy is automatically dismissed.
B) The deputy remains in office.
C) The deputy is dismissed only if the judge had no authority from the ruler.
D) The deputy must seek reappointment from the new judge.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi view is that a deputy judge is not dismissed by the death or removal of the appointing judge because he is the deputy of the ruler, not the judge.

Conditions for Valid Deputy Action Without Delegation Authority
Conditions for validity of deputy's action when principal lacks delegation authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which a deputy's action is valid when the principal lacks delegation authority, and identify which condition is disputed by Imam Zufar.

Core Idea
A deputy's action is valid if the principal is present, approves, or fixed the price, even without delegation authority.

In Hanafi qadā and agency law, a judge or agent who has not been given authority to appoint deputies (ghayr al-mufawwaḍ) may occasionally do so. The validity of the deputy's action depends on specific indicators of the principal's consent. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'āya explains three conditions that validate the deputy's act and highlights a dispute within the school about the necessity of the first agent's presence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal scenario
Understand that the ruling applies when the judge or first agent is not authorized to delegate (ghayr al-mufawwaḍ). This is the default assumption in most cases.
The opposite is mufawwaḍ, who can freely appoint deputies.
2
List the three conditions for validity
The text gives three scenarios: (1) deputy acts in presence of principal, (2) principal later approves the act, (3) principal fixed the price for the transaction.
Each condition independently makes the deputy's action valid (ṣaḥīḥ).
3
Trace the rationale for each condition
Presence: the principal's consent is inferred from his presence. Approval: subsequent ratification relates back. Price-fixing: setting the price indicates prior consent.
These rationales are derived from the principles of agency.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Zufar holds that even if the first agent is present, the deputy's action is invalid. The majority (al-Karkhī etc.) require only subsequent approval or price-fixing.
The muftā bihi is the majority view.
5
Apply to contemporary qadā practice
The passage concludes that deputies of a judge are considered his deputies from every aspect, so they are dismissed by the judge's death or dismissal. This has practical implications for continuity of judicial acts.
This clarifies the authority of deputy judges today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي غيره إن فعل نائبه عنده أو أجاز ،هو ، أو كان قَدَّرَ الثَّمَنَ في الوكالة صح ( وفي غيره إن فعل نائبه عنده أو أجاز هو، أو كان قَدَّرَ الثَّمَنَ في الوكالة صح ) : أي في غير المفوض ، يعني إذا لم يفوض إلى القاضي والوكيل أن يستخلف الغير فاستخلفا، ففعلَ النَّائب بحضور المنوب عنه صح ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِذا فعلَ ففعله ينتقل إليه ، وكذا إن فعل بغيبته فوصل الخبر إلى المنوب عنه فأجاز؛ لأنَّه إذا انضم رأيه إلى ذلك الفعل صارَ كأَنَّه فَعَلَ، وكذا إن قَدَّرَ الوكيل الأَوَّلُ الثَّمن فباشر وكيله ؛ إذ بتقدير الثمن حصل رأيه بحضوره، فيدلُّ على أنَّ النواب الآن ينعزلون بعزل القاضي وموته ؛ لأنهم نواب القاضي من وجه، فهو كالوكيل مع الموكل ، ولا يفهم أحد الآن أنه نائب السلطان ؛ ولهذا قال العلامة ابن الغرس : ونائب القاضي في زماننا ينعزلُ بعزله وبموته ، فإنّه نائبه من كلِّ وجه. انتهى ما في «الأشباه». وعليك بالانتباه، وإن شئت الاطلاع على مباحث نفيسة متعلقة بهذا المقام، فارجع إلى حاشية الحموي على «الأشباه»، وغيرها من مبسوطات الأعلام، تركناها خوفا عن تطويل الكلام. [ ١ ] قوله : إن فعل نائبه ؛ ما أمره به من نحو القضاء والنكاح والخلع والكتابة، دون الطلاق والعتاق ؛ ولهذا لم يصح ولو عند الأوَّل. كما صرح به القُهستاني)، وغيره. [٢] قوله : صح ؛ واعلم أنه ذكر من «وكالة» «الأصل»: إنه إذا وكل بغير إذنِ موكله فعقد وكيله بحضرته جاز ، ولم يشترط للجواز إجازة الوكيل الأول. وذكر في موضع آخر في «وكالة» «الأصل»: أنه إذا باع الوكيل الثاني، والوكيل الأول حاضر أو غائب، فأجاز الوكيلُ الأوّل جاز، ومنهم من جعل في المسألة روايتين ، وذكر الكرخي أن المذكور مطلقاً محمول على ما إذا أجاز الوكيل الأول، وإلى هذا ذهب عامَّةُ المشايخ ، كذا في بعض شروح «الهداية». وعند زفر : إذا جعل الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الوكيل الأول لم يجز أيضاً ، ثم ما ذكره من أن الموكل إن عيّن الثمن الذي باع به الثاني جاز، وإن لم يكن الأول حاضراً،
And in other than the case of delegation [al-mufawwaḍ], if his deputy acts in his presence, or he [the principal] approves, or he fixed the price in the agency, it is valid. That is, in other than the delegated [judge/agent]—meaning, when the judge or agent is not authorized to appoint a substitute but does so, then if the deputy acts in the presence of the principal, it is valid because his action is attributed to the principal. Similarly, if he acts in his absence and the news reaches the principal and he approves, because his opinion joins the action and it is as if he did it himself. Similarly, if the first agent fixed the price and his agent then transacts, because by fixing the price his opinion was present. This indicates that deputies nowadays are dismissed by the dismissal and death of the judge, because they are deputies of the judge from one aspect, so it is like an agent with his principal. No one nowadays understands that he is the deputy of the sultan. Hence, al-Imām Ibn al-Ghurs said: The deputy of a judge in our time is dismissed by his dismissal and death, for he is his deputy from every aspect. End from 'al-Ashbah'. You must pay attention. If you wish to see valuable discussions related to this topic, refer to 'Hāshiyat al-Ḥamawī 'alā al-Ashbāh' and other extended works of the scholars; we left them out for fear of lengthening the discourse. [1] His saying: 'if his deputy acts' — meaning in what he ordered him to do such as judging, marriage, khul', and kitābah, but not divorce or emancipation; hence these are not valid even if in the presence of the first. As clarified by al-Quhustānī and others. [2] His saying: 'it is valid' — know that it is mentioned from 'Wikālat' of 'al-Aṣl': If he authorized someone without the permission of his principal, and his agent contracts in his presence, it is permissible, and the permission of the first agent is not a condition for permissibility. And in another place in 'Wikālat' of 'al-Aṣl': If the second agent sells, and the first agent is present or absent, and the first agent approves, it is valid. Some consider there to be two narrations on the issue. Al-Karkhī said that what is mentioned absolutely is interpreted as the case where the first agent approves, and this is the view of the majority of scholars, as in some commentaries of 'al-Hidāyah'. According to Zufar: If the second agent acts in the presence of the first agent, it is also not valid. Then what he mentioned that if the principal specified the price by which the second sold, it is valid even if the first is not present.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling that when a judge or agent (who was not given authority to appoint a deputy) does appoint someone, the deputy's action is valid under three conditions: (1) The deputy acts in the presence of the principal (judge or first agent) — because the principal's presence means the action is effectively his own. (2) The deputy acts in the absence of the principal, but the principal later approves — because approval retroactively validates the act. (3) The first agent fixed the price for the sale, and the second agent executes it — because fixing the price indicates the first agent's consent. The passage then draws a practical conclusion: since contemporary deputies are considered deputies of the judge (not the ruler), they are dismissed by the judge's death or dismissal, contrary to the earlier mufta bihi view. The author then clarifies that the actions which a deputy can validly perform are limited to those the principal can do (like judging, marriage, divorce, etc.) but not actions like divorce or emancipation which require specific delegation. Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Zufar, even if the second agent acts in the presence of the first, it is invalid, but the majority (including al-Karkhī) hold it valid if the first agent approves.

Key Terms
مفوض (mufawwaḍ): One who has been given full authority to appoint deputies; opposite of ghayr al-mufawwaḍ.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent or deputy appointed by another to perform a legal act.
إجازة (ijāzah): Subsequent approval or ratification of an act.
تقدير الثمن (taqdīr al-thaman): Fixing or specifying the price by the principal, which indicates consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The deputy must be a freedman.

Key Takeaway
A deputy's action is valid without delegation authority if the principal is present, approves, or fixed the price; Zufar's stricter view is not followed.

Validity of Deputy's Action in Presence or with Ratification
Explains validity conditions for a deputy's action and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a deputy's action is valid in Hanafi law and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the necessity of ratification.

Core Idea
A deputy's act is valid when the principal is present or ratifies; ratification is the safer relied-upon view.

When a judge or agent appoints a deputy without authorization, the deputy's actions are not automatically void. The Hanafi school examines whether the principal's presence or later ratification can validate those acts. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the subtle differences among Hanafi authorities regarding when ratification is required—a distinction with practical consequences for judicial delegations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Pinpoint the act in question: a deputy appointed without prior authorization by a judge or first agent. Note that the passage deals with non-delegated agency and focuses on whether the deputy's action is valid.
2
State the Hanafi position(s) on validity
According to the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the deputy's action is valid if done in the principal's presence, or if done in absence and later ratified. However, Zufar holds that even presence is insufficient without ratification; al-Karkhi and most mashayikh require ratification in all cases.
Thus there are two strains: one view finds presence sufficient, the other requires express ratification.
3
Follow the reasoning behind each view
The majority view: presence of the principal implies tacit consent, so the action transfers to him. Ratification joins the principal's will to the act. Zufar's view: the first agent had no authority to appoint, so the deputy's act is void ab initio unless the principal later ratifies or fixes the price.
Al-Karkhi's position is that the texts mentioning 'presence' are understood as requiring ratification.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If the second agent sells while the first agent is present but does not ratify, some Hanafis (early view) consider the sale valid; Zufar and the later majority consider it invalid until ratified. The difference affects the enforceability of the contract.
For example, if a judge's deputy divorces the judge's wife in the judge's presence but without explicit consent, the divorce may be void under the preferred view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The later Hanafi authorities (al-Karkhi, most mashayikh, and commentators like al-Marghinani) adopt the view that ratification is necessary, even when the principal is present. This is now the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Students should note that the 'presence sufficient' view is a weaker narration, not used in practice.

Source Text

[٢] قوله : صح ؛ واعلم أنه ذكر من «وكالة» «الأصل»: إنه إذا وكل بغير إذنِ موكله فعقد وكيله بحضرته جاز ، ولم يشترط للجواز إجازة الوكيل الأول. وذكر في موضع آخر في «وكالة» «الأصل»: أنه إذا باع الوكيل الثاني، والوكيل الأول حاضر أو غائب، فأجاز الوكيلُ الأوّل جاز، ومنهم من جعل في المسألة روايتين ، وذكر الكرخي أن المذكور مطلقاً محمول على ما إذا أجاز الوكيل الأول، وإلى هذا ذهب عامَّةُ المشايخ ، كذا في بعض شروح «الهداية». وعند زفر : إذا جعل الوكيل الثاني بحضرة الوكيل الأول لم يجز أيضاً ، ثم ما ذكره من أن الموكل إن عيّن الثمن الذي باع به الثاني جاز، وإن لم يكن الأول حاضراً، كذا في بعض شروح «الهداية».
And in other than it (i.e., the delegated agency), if his deputy acts in his presence or he ratifies, or he had fixed the price in the agency, it is valid. That is, in the non-delegated agency, meaning when the judge and the agent were not given authority to appoint a substitute, but they did appoint, and the deputy acted in the presence of the principal, it is valid because when he acts, his action transfers to him. Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a deputy's action is valid if done in the presence of the principal or with subsequent ratification. The author distinguishes between delegated and non-delegated agency. In non-delegated agency, if a judge or agent appoints a substitute without authorization, the substitute's action is valid if done in the presence of the appointer, because the action transfers to the appointer. Similarly, if done in absence and later ratified, the appointer's approval makes it effective. Fixing the price by the first agent also validates the second's sale. This reasoning leads to the conclusion that deputies of judges in later times are considered agents of the judge, not the sultan, so they are dismissed by the judge's dismissal or death. The commentary then provides two footnotes: the first specifies that the deputy's authority covers acts like adjudication, marriage, etc., but not divorce or manumission; the second discusses variations in Hanafi rulings on whether the second agent's act is valid without ratification. Some scholars hold two narrations, but al-Karkhi favors the opinion that ratification is necessary, which is accepted by most. Zufar holds that even with the first agent present, the second's act is invalid unless the principal specifies the price.

Key Terms
نائب (na'ib): A deputy appointed by the judge or the first agent to perform a specific act.
المنوب عنه (al-manubu 'anhu): The principal for whom the deputy acts, i.e., the judge or the first agent.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or approval of an act done by the deputy without prior authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is valid only if the deputy is appointed directly by the sultan.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a deputy's unauthorized action is valid if the principal later ratifies it—this is the relied-upon position ensuring legal certainty.

Effect of 'Act on Your Opinion': Delegation and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Enforcement
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation and enforcement under 'act on your opinion'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's ability to delegate when told 'act on your opinion' and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute on enforcement of the deputy's judgment.

Core Idea
'Act on your opinion' allows delegation to a substitute who becomes the principal's direct agent; but enforcement of the deputy's judgment is disputed.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya addresses a key phrase in agency: when a principal says 'act on your opinion' (i'mal bi ra'yik). It clarifies that the agent may appoint a substitute, and that substitute becomes the principal's agent directly. However, the enforcement of any judgment given by that deputy is subject to an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf upholds enforcement, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
When the principal says 'i'mal bi ra'yik', the agent may appoint a substitute. This ruling is unanimous among the Hanafi imams.
The substitute becomes the principal's agent, not the first agent's agent.
2
Determine the legal effect on the substitute's tenure
The substitute is not dismissed by the dismissal or death of the first agent; both are dismissed only by the death of the principal.
This confirms the substitute is directly the principal's agent.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on enforcement
The phrase 'yamdi hukm' refers to the enforcement of the deputy's judgment. Abu Yusuf permits enforcement; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a second judge presented with the deputy's judgment will enforce it only if following Abu Yusuf's opinion.
4
Note the authority and tarjih
Al-Birjandi reports from al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyyah. The delegation part is mufta bihi; the dispute is recorded without explicit tarjih in this passage.
Later works may resolve the tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباعمل برأيك يُوكل غيره، ويُمْضى حكم (إنما هو قول أبي يوسف الله، وأما عند محمد الله فلا يجوز، وهو قول أبي حنيفة . كذا ذكره البرْجَندِي له ناقلا عن «الفتاوى المنصورية».) (١) قوله : يوكل غيره؛ ويكون الغير وكيلاً عن الموكل، وكذا لا ينعزل الثاني بعزل الأول ولا بموته، وكلاهما ينعزلان بموت الموكل.
And with 'act on your opinion', he may appoint someone else, and his judgment will be enforced. (This is only the saying of Abu Yusuf, may Allah be pleased with him, but according to Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, it is not permissible, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa. Thus al-Birjandi mentioned it, reporting from 'al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyyah'.) (1) His saying: 'he may appoint someone else' means that the other becomes an agent for the principal, and similarly the second is not dismissed by the dismissal or death of the first, and both are dismissed by the death of the principal.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of the principal saying to the agent 'act on your opinion' (i'mal bi ra'yik). According to the text, this phrase allows the agent to appoint another person as his substitute. The note (1) clarifies that the substitute becomes an agent of the original principal, not of the first agent. Therefore, the substitute is not dismissed if the first agent is dismissed or dies; both remain agents until the principal dies. The main sentence also mentions 'and his judgment will be enforced' (yamdi hukm), but the intra-Hanafi dispute about that is covered in the next lesson. The parenthetical statement indicates that the enforcement of the judgment (by a second judge) is according to Abu Yusuf only, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not permit it. This lesson focuses specifically on the delegation aspect.

Key Terms
اعمل برأيك (i'mal bi ra'yik): A phrase said by the principal to the agent meaning 'act according to your own judgment', which broadens the agent's authority.
يوكل غيره (yuwakkil ghayrahu): The agent appoints a substitute who becomes the direct agent of the original principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a principal tells his agent 'act on your opinion', what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the agent's ability to appoint a substitute?
A) The agent may not appoint a substitute; it must be done personally.
B) The agent may appoint a substitute, but the substitute is the first agent's agent, not the principal's.
C) The agent may appoint a substitute, and the substitute becomes the principal's agent directly.
D) The agent may appoint a substitute only with explicit permission from the principal.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'act on your opinion' grants the agent authority to delegate, and the substitute is directly the principal's agent; however, enforcement of the substitute's judgment is disputed among the Hanafi imams.

Enforcement of Judgments by a Second Judge
Categorizes judgments by evidence type and ijtihad type, with enforcement rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize a first judge's ruling based on its evidentiary basis (nass mufassar or ijtihad) and determine whether a second judge must enforce, may overturn, or must reject it.

Core Idea
A second judge enforces rulings based on explicit texts and substantive ijtihad, but may overturn procedural ijtihad rulings.

Judges are not infallible, but Islamic jurisprudence respects ijtihad. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the precise rules for when a second judge can review a first judge's decision. Understanding these categories is essential for anyone studying judicial authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of judgments
The first judge's ruling is either based on an explicit text (nass mufassar from Quran, Mutawatir Sunnah, or Ijma) or on ijtihad (from apparent meanings and analogy).
Explicit texts are qat'i (definitive), while ijtihad relies on zanni (probable) evidence.
2
Determine the second judge's duty for explicit-text rulings
If the ruling agrees with the explicit text or ijma, the second judge must enforce and cannot overturn. If it contradicts, he must reject it.
No ijtihad is allowed against a nass mufassar.
3
Break down the ijtihad category into subcategories
Ijtihad matters are either unanimously agreed to be ijtihad or disputed. The passage focuses on the unanimous case, which splits further into substantive vs. procedural ijtihad.
Substantive: the outcome of the case (e.g., validity of a sale). Procedural: the permissibility of adjudicating at all (e.g., judging on an absent person).
4
Apply the rule for substantive ijtihad
If the substance is the subject of ijtihad, the second judge must enforce the first's ruling. If he overturns, a third judge restores the first's judgment.
This upholds the principle 'al-ijtihad la yunqad bi al-ijtihad' (ijtihad is not invalidated by another ijtihad).
5
Apply the rule for procedural ijtihad
If the act of judging itself is subject to ijtihad (e.g., whether to hear a case against an absent person), the second judge may overturn if his ijtihad differs.
This respects the second judge's ijtihad on jurisdictional matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) قوله : ويمضي حكم؛ تلخيص الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام : إن قضاء القاضي الأول لا يخلو : ١ . إمّا أن يقع في فصل فيه نص مفسر من الكتاب والسنة المتواترة، أو إجماع. ٢. وإما أن يقع في فصل مجتهد فيه من ظواهر النصوص والقياس. فإن وقع في فصل فيه مفسر من الكتاب والخبر المتواتر، أو إجماع، فإن وافق قضاؤه ذلك نفذه الثاني، ولا يحل له النقض، وإن خالف شيئاً من ذلك ردَّه. وإن وقع في فصل مجتهد فيه فلا يخلو : ١ . إمّا إن كان مجمعا على كونه مجتهدا فيه. ٢. وإما إن كان مختلفاً في كونه مجتهداً فيه. فإن كان مجمعاً على كونه محل الاجتهاد : .١. فإما إن كان المجتهد هو المقضي به. ٢. وإما إن كان نفس القضاء. فإن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضى به، فرفع قضاءه إلى قاض آخر، لم يرده الثاني بل يُنفذه ، فإن رده القاضي الثاني فرجع إلى قاض ثالث نفد قضاء القاضي الأول، وأبطل قضاء الثاني. وإن كان نفس القضاء مجتهداً فيه أنه يجوز أم لا ، كما لو قضى بالحجر على الحرّ، أو قضى على الغائب، يجوز للقاضي الثاني أن ينقض الأوّل إذا مال اجتهاده إلى خلافِ اجتهاد الأول، هذا إذا كان القضاء في محل أجمعوا على كونه محل الاجتهاد.
(2) His saying: 'and his judgment is enforced' – summary of the discourse to clarify the objective: The judgment of the first judge is not free from: 1. Either it falls under a matter in which there is an explicit text from the Book, the Mutawatir Sunnah, or consensus. 2. Or it falls under a matter open to ijtihad from the apparent meanings of texts and analogy. If it falls under a matter in which there is an explicit text from the Book and Mutawatir Hadith, or consensus, then if his judgment agrees with that, the second judge enforces it and it is not permissible for him to overturn it. And if it contradicts any of that, he rejects it. And if it falls under a matter open to ijtihad, then it is not free from: 1. Either it is agreed that it is a matter of ijtihad. 2. Or there is disagreement on whether it is a matter of ijtihad. If it is agreed that it is a place of ijtihad: (a) Either the matter being adjudicated is the subject of ijtihad, (b) Or the judgment itself is the subject of ijtihad. If the matter adjudicated is the subject of ijtihad, then if his judgment is brought to another judge, the second does not reject it but enforces it. And if the second judge rejects it and it goes to a third judge, the first judge's judgment prevails and the second's is voided. And if the judgment itself is subject to ijtihad regarding whether it is permissible or not, such as if he ruled for interdiction over a free man, or judged against an absent person, then the second judge may overturn the first if his ijtihad leans to the contrary of the first's ijtihad. This is when the judgment is in a matter agreed to be a place of ijtihad.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of when a second judge must enforce or may overturn the ruling of a first judge. The author categorizes judgments into two types: those based on explicit texts (nass mufassar from Quran, Mutawatir Sunnah, or Ijma) and those based on ijtihad. For the first type, the second judge must enforce if it agrees with the texts, and must reject if it contradicts. For the second type (ijtihad), there are subcategories: if it is unanimously agreed to be an area of ijtihad, then if the substantive ruling (the outcome) is the subject of ijtihad, the second judge must enforce it and cannot overturn; if he does overturn, a third judge would revert to the first. If the very act of making the judgment in that case is what is subject to ijtihad (e.g., whether the judge had jurisdiction to rule on an absent person), then the second judge may overturn if his ijtihad differs. This distinction ensures respect for ijtihad while allowing correction of procedural overreach.

Key Terms
نص مفسر (nass mufassar): An explicit text from the Quran or Mutawatir Sunnah with clear and unambiguous meaning.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fih): A matter that requires ijtihad because there is no definitive text; it is open to scholarly interpretation.
المقضى به (al-muqda bih): The substantive outcome of the judgment, e.g., the ruling on the sale's validity.
نفس القضاء (nfs al-qada): The act of adjudication itself, including its permissibility and procedural aspects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a second judge do if the first judge's ruling is based on a matter unanimously agreed to be ijtihad, and the substance of the ruling is the subject of ijtihad?
A) He must enforce the ruling and cannot overturn it.
B) He may overturn it based on his own ijtihad.
C) He must reject the ruling because it is not based on definitive evidence.
D) He should refer the case to a third judge for a final decision.

Key Takeaway
A second judge must enforce rulings based on explicit texts and substantive ijtihad, but may overturn procedural ijtihad rulings if his ijtihad differs.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Agent's Delegation When Told 'Act by Your Opinion'
Clarifying the ruling on delegation when a principal says 'act by your opinion'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether an agent can delegate when told 'act by your opinion,' and identify the authority for each position.

Core Idea
Even with 'act by your opinion,' delegation is permissible only according to Abu Yusuf.

This passage addresses a subtle point in agency law: does a principal's instruction 'act by your opinion' grant the agent authority to appoint a substitute? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this question. Understanding this dispute clarifies the limits of discretionary agency and the importance of explicit permission for delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principal's Wording
The principal says 'اعمل برأيك' (act by your opinion). What does this phrase imply about the agent's authority? Does it include the power to delegate?
2
Recall the Three Imams' Positions
Abu Yusuf holds it permits delegation; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not. Note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree here.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Recognize the Source of the Ruling
The passage cites al-Burjandi quoting al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya. This is from later fatwa compilations, not the early Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if the agent delegates without permission, is the sub-agent's act valid? Under Abu Yusuf, yes; under the other two, no. The principal may not be bound by the sub-agent's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويا عمل برأيك يُوكل غيره ) : أي إذا قال الموكل للوكيل : اعمل برأيك كان للوكيل أن يوكل غيره. ويُمْضى حكم) إنما هو قول أبي يوسف [الله؟] ، وأما عند محمد [الله؟] فلا يجوز، وهو قول أبي حنيفة . كذا ذكره البرْجَندِي له ناقلا عن «الفتاوى المنصورية».
(And act by your opinion, he delegates another): That is, when the principal says to the agent: act by your opinion, the agent has the right to delegate another. And his judgment is executed) This is only the saying of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, but according to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, it is not permissible, and it is the saying of Abu Hanifa. Thus mentioned by al-Burjandi, quoting from al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the scope of an agent's authority. The matn phrase 'act by your opinion, he delegates another' means that if a principal gives broad discretion to an agent by saying 'act by your opinion,' the agent may delegate the task to a third party. The commentator clarifies that this ruling is specifically the position of Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Hanifa hold that such delegation is not permitted, even with that wording. The authority quoted is al-Burjandi, who cites al-Fatawa al-Mansuriyya. The intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Yusuf allows the agent to appoint a sub-agent, while the other two Imams do not. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is not specified here, but later Hanafi fatwa manuals may prefer Abu Yusuf's view in certain contexts.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed by a principal (muwakkil) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
يوكل غيره (yuwakkil ghayrahu): To appoint another person as sub-agent to perform the task originally assigned to the first agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The view is unanimous

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of an agent's delegation when told 'act by your opinion' is disputed in the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf allows it, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not.

Enforcement and Annulment of Judgments by a Second Judge
Ruling with proof on second judge's authority to enforce or annul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between judgments based on explicit texts and ijtihad, and apply the Hanafi rules for when a second judge must enforce, may annul, or must reject a prior judgment.

Core Idea
A second judge cannot overrule a judgment based on explicit text or ijma, but may annul ijtihad-based rulings on certain conditions.

In the Hanafi judicial system, the finality of a judge's ruling is not absolute. The second judge has specific obligations and limitations when a previous judgment is brought before him. This lesson unpacks the nuanced criteria that determine whether enforcement is mandatory or whether annulment is permitted, based on the nature of the evidence behind the first ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories of judgments
Determine whether the first judge's ruling was based on explicit text (nass mufassar, mutawatir, or ijma) or on ijtihad (from apparent meanings and analogy). This determines the second judge's initial action.
2
Apply the rule for explicit text-based judgments
If the ruling conforms to the explicit text, the second judge must enforce it and cannot annul. If it contradicts, he must reject it. This is rooted in the binding nature of definitive evidence.
Example: a ruling permitting marriage to a father's wife contradicts Quran 4:22 and must be rejected.
3
For ijtihad-based rulings, determine if the ijtihad is agreed upon or disputed
If the matter is unanimously recognized as ijtihad, proceed to step 4. If there is disagreement (e.g., umm walad), the second judge follows his own assessment.
Unanimity is judged by the consensus of the early period (al-sadr al-awwal).
4
Distinguish between ijtihad on the subject matter and ijtihad on the judgment itself
If the ijtihad concerns the subject matter (mahkum bih), the second judge must enforce and cannot annul; if he does annul, a third judge reinstates the first. If the ijtihad concerns the judgment's permissibility (e.g., ruling against absent party), the second judge may annul based on his own ijtihad.
The former is like a ruling on the validity of a sale; the latter is like a ruling on procedural permissibility.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute and the ruling against an absent party
Note Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad on selling umm walad, and the two narrations on ruling against an absent person. Identify the mufta bihi (Muhammad's view and non-execution of absent party ruling).
The sahih according to al-Khassaf and al-Sarakhsi is that an absent party judgment is not executed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُمْضى حكم) تلخيص الكلام بحيث ينكشف به المرام : إن قضاء القاضي الأول لا يخلو : ١ . إمّا أن يقع في فصل فيه نص مفسر من الكتاب والسنة المتواترة، أو إجماع. ٢. وإما أن يقع في فصل مجتهد فيه من ظواهر النصوص والقياس. فإن وقع في فصل فيه مفسر من الكتاب والخبر المتواتر، أو إجماع، فإن وافق قضاؤه ذلك نفذه الثاني، ولا يحل له النقض، وإن خالف شيئاً من ذلك ردَّه. وإن وقع في فصل مجتهد فيه فلا يخلو : ١ . إمّا إن كان مجمعا على كونه مجتهدا فيه. ٢. وإما إن كان مختلفاً في كونه مجتهداً فيه. فإن كان مجمعاً على كونه محل الاجتهاد : ١. فإما إن كان المجتهد هو المقضي به. ٢. وإما إن كان نفس القضاء. فإن كان المجتهد فيه هو المقضى به، فرفع قضاءه إلى قاض آخر، لم يرده الثاني بل يُنفذه ، فإن رده القاضي الثاني فرجع إلى قاض ثالث نفد قضاء القاضي الأول، وأبطل قضاء الثاني. وإن كان نفس القضاء مجتهداً فيه أنه يجوز أم لا ، كما لو قضى بالحجر على الحرّ، أو قضى على الغائب، يجوز للقاضي الثاني أن ينقض الأوّل إذا مال اجتهاده إلى خلافِ اجتهاد الأول، هذا إذا كان القضاء في محل أجمعوا على كونه محل الاجتهاد. قاض آخر في مختلف فيه في الصدر الأَوَّل إلا ما خالف الكتاب فأما إذا كان في محل اختلفوا أنه محل الاجتهاد أم لا: كبيع أم الولد أنه هل ينفذ فيه قضاء القاضي عند أبي حنيفة الله وأبي يوسف : ينفذ ؛ لأنه محل الاجتهاد عندهما ؛ لاختلاف الصحابة له في جواز بيعها. وعند محمد ه : لا ينفذ لوقوع الاتفاق بعد ذلك من الصحابة وغيرهم، على أنه لا يجوز بيعها ، فخرج محل الاجتهاد، فينظر إن كان من رأي القاضي الثاني أنه مجتهد فيه ينفذ قضاؤه ولا يرده، وإن كان في رأيه أنه خرج من حد الاجتهاد وصار متفقاً عليه لا ينفذ، بل يردّه. كذا في «البدائع». وإذا كان نفس القضاء مختلفاً فيه بأن قضى القاضي بحق على الغائب أو للغائب ، هل ينفذ ؟ فيه روايتان عن أصحابنا في رواية لا ينفذ هكذا ذكر الخصاف، وهو الصحيح، كذا في «محيط السرخسي»، ذكره في «الفتاوى العالمكيرية»(٢). (1) قوله : قاض آخر ; خرج به المحكم ، فإنّه إذا رفع حكمه إلى قاض آخر أمضاه إن وافق مذهبه، وإلا أبطله؛ لأنَّ حكمه لا يرفع خلافاً، ودخل فيه الميت والمعزول وقاضي البغاة، وغيرهم ممن يمضي حكمه. [٢] قوله : في الصدر الأول ; قيل : هو زمانُ الصحابة والتابعين ، وقيل : المراد ما يعم من الصحابة والفقهاء المجتهدين . كذا في «مجمع الأنهر )) (-4) [٣] قوله : ما خالف الكتاب ; والمراد من مخالفة الكتاب مخالفة نص الكتاب الذي لم يختلف السلف في تأويله، كقوله : ( وَلَا تَنكِحُوا مَا نَكَعَ مَابَاؤُكُم مِّن النِّسَاءِ (٤) ، فإنَّ السلف اتَّفقوا على عدم جواز تزويج امرأة الأب وجاريته ووطئها إن وطأها الأب، فلو حكم حاكم بجواز ذلك نقضه من رفع إليه. كذا في «العناية».
(And his judgment is executed) Summary of the speech so that the goal is revealed: The judgment of the first judge is not free: 1. Either it occurs in a matter concerning which there is an explicit text from the Book and the Mutawatir Sunnah, or Ijma. 2. Or it occurs in a matter of ijtihad from apparent meanings of texts and analogy. If it occurs in a matter concerning which there is an explicit text from the Book and the Mutawatir report, or Ijma, then if his judgment agreed with that, the second judge executes it, and it is not permissible for him to annul it; and if it opposed any of that, he rejects it. And if it occurs in a matter of ijtihad, then it is not free: 1. Either it is agreed upon that it is a matter of ijtihad. 2. Or there is disagreement whether it is a matter of ijtihad. If it is agreed upon that it is a place of ijtihad: 1. Either the ijtihad is concerning the subject matter of the judgment. 2. Or concerning the judgment itself. If the ijtihad is concerning the subject matter, and his judgment is raised to another judge, the second does not reject it but executes it. If the second judge rejects it and it goes to a third judge, the judgment of the first is executed and the judgment of the second is nullified. If the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad whether it is permissible or not, as if he ruled on restriction of a free person, or ruled against an absent person, then the second judge may annul the first if his ijtihad leans contrary to the ijtihad of the first. This is when the judgment is in a place agreed to be a place of ijtihad. A second judge in a matter disputed in the early period except what contradicts the Book. As for when it is in a place where they differed whether it is a place of ijtihad or not: like the sale of an umm walad – does the judgment of a judge execute it? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it executes, because it is a place of ijtihad in their view due to the disagreement of the Companions about the permissibility of selling her. According to Muhammad: it does not execute because after that there occurred agreement from the Companions and others that it is not permissible to sell her, so it exits the realm of ijtihad. So consider: if in the opinion of the second judge it is a matter of ijtihad, he executes the judgment and does not reject it; if in his opinion it has left the realm of ijtihad and become agreed upon, he does not execute but rejects it. This is as in al-Bada'i'. And if the judgment itself is disputed, such as a judge ruling in favor of or against an absent person, is it executed? There are two narrations from our companions; in one narration it is not executed. Thus mentioned al-Khassaf, and it is the sahih, as in Muhit al-Sarakhsi, mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya. (1) His saying: another judge; excludes the arbitrator (muhakkam), for if his judgment is raised to another judge, he executes it if it agrees with his madhhab, otherwise he nullifies it, because his judgment does not remove ikhtilaf. And includes the deceased, the removed judge, and the judge of rebels, and others whose judgment is executed. [2] His saying: in the early period (al-sadr al-awwal); it is said: it is the time of the Companions and the Followers; and it is said: what is meant is that which includes the Companions and the mujtahid jurists. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. [3] His saying: except what contradicts the Book; and what is meant by contradicting the Book is contradicting the text of the Book upon which the predecessors did not differ in its interpretation, like His saying: 'And do not marry what your fathers married from among women' (4:22); for the predecessors agreed on the impermissibility of marrying the father's wife and his concubine and having intercourse with her if the father had intercourse with her. So if a judge rules on the permissibility of that, whoever it is brought to annuls it. Thus in al-'Inaya.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the enforcement of judgments by a second judge. The author summarizes the principles: The first judge's ruling falls into two categories: (1) Based on explicit text (nass mufassar) from the Quran, mutawatir sunnah, or ijma. In this case, the second judge must enforce it and cannot annul it if it conforms; if it contradicts, he must reject it. (2) Based on ijtihad (legal reasoning from apparent meanings and analogy). This category further subdivides: (a) Matters unanimously recognized as ijtihad. Within this, if the ijtihad concerns the subject matter (mahkum bih), the second judge must enforce it; if he rejects it and it goes to a third judge, the first judgment prevails and the second's is nullified. If the ijtihad concerns the judgment itself (e.g., whether a ruling of interdiction (hajr) against a free person is valid, or a ruling against an absent party), then the second judge may annul if his ijtihad differs. (b) Matters disputed as to whether they are ijtihad (e.g., sale of umm walad): Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it ijtihad (due to Companion disagreement) so the judgment is enforced; Muhammad holds that subsequent consensus removed it from ijtihad, so the judgment is not enforced. The second judge follows his own opinion. Additionally, the passage addresses rulings against an absent party: there are two narrations, with the sahih being that such a ruling is not executed. Marginal notes clarify terms: 'another judge' excludes arbitrators (muhakkim) whose judgment can be annulled if it disagrees with the second judge's madhhab; 'al-sadr al-awwal' refers to the time of Companions and Followers (or includes later mujtahids); 'contradicting the Book' means contradicting a clear Quranic text whose interpretation was agreed upon by the Salaf, e.g., the prohibition of marrying one's father's wife or concubine.

Key Terms
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fīh): A matter open to ijtihad (legal reasoning) because no definitive text exists, or texts are open to interpretation.
الصدر الأول (al-ṣadr al-awwal): The early period of Islam including the Companions (Sahabah) and the Followers (Tabi'in), or more broadly including later mujtahid jurists.
ما خالف الكتاب (mā khālafa al-kitāb): Any ruling that contradicts an explicit Quranic text whose interpretation was unanimously agreed upon by the early scholars (Salaf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a first judge rules on a matter that is agreed upon to be ijtihad (like the validity of a judgment against an absent person), what is the ruling for the second judge?
A) He must enforce it without question.
B) He may annul it if his ijtihad differs from the first judge's.
C) He must annul it regardless of his ijtihad.
D) He must refer it to a third judge.

Key Takeaway
The second judge's authority is bounded by the nature of the first judgment: explicit texts demand enforcement, while ijtihad-based judgments allow annulment only when the ijtihad concerns the judgment itself or the ijtihad status is disputed.

Enforcement of a Prior Judge's Verdict in Disputed Ijtihad Matters
Intra-Hanafi dispute on enforcing a prior judge's ruling in disputed matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that a second judge must enforce a prior verdict unless it contradicts a clear text, and apply the criterion of whether the issue remains open to ijtihad to determine enforceability in cases like the sale of umm al-walad.

Core Idea
A second judge enforces a prior verdict unless it contradicts a definitive text; intra-madhhab disagreement arises when later consensus removes an issue from ijtihad.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a critical question in Hanafi judicial procedure: when may a second judge overturn the ruling of a prior judge? The general rule is enforcement unless clear text or binding consensus is violated. However, a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute emerges regarding matters that were once open to ijtihad but later became settled by consensus. The sale of umm al-walad serves as the classic example, and the passage also addresses the separate issue of rulings on absent parties.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule and Exceptions
Identify the default obligation of the second judge: enforce the prior verdict. Then note the sole exception: when the verdict contradicts a clear, unequivocal text from the Quran, well-known Sunnah, or binding consensus.
The exception is narrow; mere difference of ijtihad does not justify overturning.
2
Analyze the Umm al-Walad Example
Focus on the specific intra-Hanafi dispute over the sale of umm al-walad. Compare the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf (enforce, because it is a matter of ijtihad) and Imam Muhammad (do not enforce, because later consensus removed it from ijtihad).
The second judge's own view determines whether he considers the matter still open or settled.
3
Examine the Absent-Party Issue
Consider the secondary dispute: if the prior ruling itself (e.g., against an absent party) is contested, there are two narrations. The correct view is that it does not take effect.
This shows another layer of dispute within the school.
4
Define Key Terms
Understand the definitions provided in the commentary: 'another judge' excludes the arbitrator; 'the first era' is the time of Companions and Successors; 'contradicts the Book' means contradicting a verse with an agreed-upon interpretation.
These definitions delimit the scope of the rule.
5
Synthesize the Decision Process
Formulate a step-by-step procedure for a second judge: examine the ruling, check if it contradicts a clear text, assess whether the issue remains open to ijtihad, then enforce or overturn based on his own conviction.
The mufta bihi position leaves the final decision to the second judge's ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاض آخر في مختلف فيه في الصدر الأَوَّل إلا ما خالف الكتاب
فأما إذا كان في محل اختلفوا أنه محل الاجتهاد أم لا: كبيع أم الولد أنه هل ينفذ فيه قضاء القاضي عند أبي حنيفة الله وأبي يوسف : ينفذ ؛ لأنه محل الاجتهاد عندهما ؛ لاختلاف الصحابة له في جواز بيعها.
وعند محمد ه : لا ينفذ لوقوع الاتفاق بعد ذلك من الصحابة وغيرهم، على أنه لا يجوز بيعها ، فخرج محل الاجتهاد، فينظر إن كان من رأي القاضي الثاني أنه مجتهد فيه ينفذ قضاؤه ولا يرده، وإن كان في رأيه أنه خرج من حد الاجتهاد وصار متفقاً عليه لا ينفذ، بل يردّه. كذا في «البدائع». وإذا كان نفس القضاء مختلفاً فيه بأن قضى القاضي بحق على الغائب أو للغائب ، هل ينفذ ؟ فيه روايتان عن أصحابنا في رواية لا ينفذ هكذا ذكر الخصاف، وهو الصحيح، كذا في «محيط السرخسي»، ذكره في «الفتاوى العالمكيرية».
[١] قوله : قاض آخر ؛ خرج به المحكم ، فإنّه إذا رفع حكمه إلى قاض آخر أمضاه إن وافق مذهبه، وإلا أبطله؛ لأنَّ حكمه لا يرفع خلافاً، ودخل فيه الميت والمعزول وقاضي البغاة، وغيرهم ممن يمضي حكمه.
[٢] قوله : في الصدر الأول ؛ قيل : هو زمانُ الصحابة والتابعين ، وقيل : المراد ما يعم من الصحابة والفقهاء المجتهدين . كذا في «مجمع الأنهر».
[٣] قوله : ما خالف الكتاب ؛ والمراد من مخالفة الكتاب مخالفة نص الكتاب الذي لم يختلف السلف في تأويله، كقوله : ( وَلَا تَنكِحُوا مَا نَكَعَ مَابَاؤُكُم مِّن النِّسَاءِ ، فإنَّ السلف اتَّفقوا على عدم جواز تزويج امرأة الأب وجاريته ووطئها إن وطأها الأب، فلو حكم حاكم بجواز ذلك نقضه من رفع إليه. كذا في «العناية».
Another judge in a disputed matter in the first era except what contradicts the Book. As for when it is a place of ijtihad or not: such as the sale of umm al-walad, does the judge's ruling there take effect? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it takes effect because it is a place of ijtihad in their view due to the disagreement of the Companions regarding the permissibility of selling her. According to Muhammad: it does not take effect because agreement occurred after that among the Companions and others that selling her is not permissible, so it left the scope of ijtihad. He sees: if the second judge is of the opinion that it is a matter of ijtihad, his ruling takes effect and he does not reject it; but if in his view it has left the scope of ijtihad and become agreed upon, it does not take effect; rather he rejects it. Thus in al-Bada'i. And if the ruling itself is disputed, such as when a judge renders a verdict regarding an absent person or for an absent person, does it take effect? There are two narrations from our companions: in one narration it does not take effect; al-Khassaf mentioned it, and that is the correct view. Thus in Muhit al-Sarakhsi, mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya. [1] His saying: 'another judge' excludes the arbitrator. If his ruling is brought to another judge, he enforces it if it agrees with his madhhab, otherwise he nullifies it, because his ruling does not remove disagreement. Included are the deceased, the removed, the judge of rebels, and others whose verdicts are enforced. [2] His saying: 'in the first era' means the time of the Companions and Successors, and it is said what includes the Companions and the jurist-mujtahids. Thus in Majma' al-Anhar. [3] His saying: 'what contradicts the Book' means contradicting a clear text of the Book whose interpretation the predecessors did not differ over, such as His saying: 'Do not marry what your fathers married of women.' The predecessors agreed on the impermissibility of marrying the father's wife and his slave-girl and having intercourse with her if the father had intercourse with her. So if a judge ruled on its permissibility, whoever has it brought to him would overturn it. Thus in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the enforceability of a prior judge's ruling by a second judge in matters where ijtihad is possible. The general rule is that the second judge must enforce the first judge's verdict unless it contradicts a clear text from the Quran, well-known Sunnah, or binding consensus. However, when the matter itself is disputed (different ijtihad), the second judge's ruling depends on whether he considers the issue still open to ijtihad or settled by later consensus. The specific example is the sale of a child's mother (umm al-walad). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the prior judge's ruling permitting sale should be enforced because the Companions themselves differed on this issue, so it remains a valid domain of ijtihad. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that later Companions and successors reached consensus that selling umm al-walad is impermissible, thus removing it from ijtihad. Therefore, if the second judge agrees with Muhammad, he may overturn the earlier ruling. The passage then mentions a separate but related issue: if the prior ruling itself (e.g., against an absent party) is disputed, there are two narrations within the school, with the correct view being that it does not take effect. The commentary further clarifies that 'another judge' excludes arbitrators (who lack authority to settle disputes finally), and defines 'the first era' as the time of the Companions and Successors. Finally, 'what contradicts the Book' means contradicting a verse with unambiguous, agreed-upon interpretation, illustrated by the prohibition of marrying the father's wife.

Key Terms
محل الاجتهاد (mahall al-ijtihad): A legal issue on which qualified jurists may differ based on valid evidence.
بيع أم الولد (bay' umm al-walad): The sale of a female slave who has borne a child to her master.
الصدر الأول (al-sadr al-awwal): The first era, interpreted as the time of the Companions and Successors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, why is a prior judge's verdict on the sale of umm al-walad not enforceable by a second judge?
A) Because the sale is invalid under all circumstances.
B) Because later consensus removed the issue from ijtihad.
C) Because the first judge lacked authority.
D) Because the second judge has superior ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
A second judge must enforce a prior verdict unless it contradicts a clear text or binding consensus; within ijtihad matters, the second judge's own view determines enforceability.

Exceptions to Enforcing a Prior Judge's Ruling: Well-Known Sunnah and Ijma
Defining exceptions to enforcing a prior verdict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'well-known Sunnah' and 'ijma' in the context of overturning a judicial verdict, and explain the rationale for enforcing prior rulings.

Core Idea
Only clear, well-established texts (well-known Sunnah or Ijma) can overturn a judge's verdict; rare hadiths or innovations cannot.

This passage continues the discussion of when a subsequent judge must enforce a prior verdict. It defines the two exceptions that allow nullification: contradiction to the well-known Sunnah (not rare reports) or to valid consensus. The author explains why 'well-known' is specified, how consensus is properly defined, and the practical reasoning behind enforcing prior rulings to prevent endless reversals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions
Read the condition 'إلا أن يكون مخالفاً لهما' — the exceptions are contradiction to the Book, well-known Sunnah, or Ijma. Note that 'Book' and 'Sunnah' are qualified.
The text specifies 'or the well-known Sunnah, or Ijma' as the exceptions.
2
Analyze 'well-known' (mashhurah)
The commentator explains that 'well-known' excludes rare (ghareeb) hadiths. Contradicting even a well-known hadith prevents enforcement a fortiori if it were mutawatir.
The restriction is to avoid weak or isolated reports overturning a ruling.
3
Understand the text's level of definitiveness
The passage clarifies that the Book and Sunnah referred to are those that are not qat'i al-dalalah (definitively indicative). Opposing a qat'i text is kufr, not a mere judicial error.
Only zanni texts are in view here; qat'i texts are in a different category.
4
Define Ijma as intended
The passage states that Ijma means consensus without a valid legal proof (dalil) supporting a dissenting opinion. Ijma that is opposed by a valid proof is not binding.
This reflects the Hanafi view that Ijma is a source only when no valid opposing proof exists.
5
Grasp the rationale for enforcement
The author provides a chain of reasoning: no ijtihad is inherently superior; the first ruling is favored by being executed, and overturning it would lead to infinite reversals. The example of Umar and Abu al-Darda' illustrates that judges' opinions may differ but the first stands.
The principle ensures finality and stability in judicial decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو السنة المشهورة، أو الإجماع يكون : أي إذا قضى القاضي ورَفَعَ حُكْمَهُ إلى قاض آخرَ يَجِبُ عليه إمضاؤه إلا أن يكون مخالفاً لهما
[١] قوله : أو السنة المشهورة ؛ قيَّد بالمشهورة احترازاً عن الغريب.
فإن قلت : خرج به المتواترة أيضاً مع أن ما خالفها لا يمضي أيضاً.
قلت : لما ذكر أنَّ ما خالف السنة المشهورة لا يمضي مع كونها أدنى درجة من المتواترة، ففي المتواترة بالطريق الأولى، والمراد من الكتاب والسنة ما لا يكون قطعي الدلالة ، فإنّ مخالفة قطعي الدلالة كفر، صرح به الفقهاء.
[٢] قوله : أو الإجماع ؛ والمراد منه ما ليس فيه خلاف يستند إلى دليل شرعي. كذا في «البحر».
[٣] قوله : يجب عليه إمضاؤه ؛ لأنه لا مزية لأحد الاجتهادين على الآخر، وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما هو دونه ؛ ولأنه لو لم ينفذ الأول لما نفذ الثاني أيضاً، وكذا الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى الاحتمال أن يجيء قاض يرمي خلاف ذلك ، فكان نافذاً ضرورة. وقد صح أنَّ عمر له لما كثر اشتغاله قلّد القضاء أبا الدرداء ، واختصم إليه رجلان، فقضى لأحدهما ثم رأى فسأله المقضي عليه ، فقال عمر : لو كنت أنا مكانه لقضيت لك ، فقال المقضي عليه : ما يمنعك من القضاء ، قال : ليس هنا نص والرأي مشتركة، وروي عن عمر أنه قضى في حادثة بقضية ثم قضى فيها بخلاف ذلك، فقيل له في ذلك ، فقال : تلك كما قضينا، وهذا كما نقضي. ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز».
Or the well-known Sunnah, or consensus. Meaning: If a judge passes a verdict and his ruling is raised to another judge, it is obligatory upon him to enforce it except if it contradicts these two. [1] His saying: 'or the well-known Sunnah' – he restricted it to 'well-known' to exclude the rare (ghareeb). If you say: 'This also excludes the mutawatir, even though contradicting it also does not enforce.' I respond: He mentioned that what contradicts the well-known Sunnah does not enforce even though it is lower in degree than mutawatir; so for mutawatir, it is axiomatic. What is meant by the Book and the Sunnah is what is not definitively indicative (qat'i al-dalalah), for opposing definitively indicative is kufr, as stated by the jurists. [2] His saying: 'or consensus' – what is meant is that there is no disagreement based on a valid legal proof. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His saying: 'it is obligatory upon him to enforce it' – because there is no preference for one ijtihad over another, and the first ijtihad has been favored by being connected to the ruling, so it is not overturned by something inferior. If the first were not enforced, the second also would not be enforced, and likewise the third and fourth to infinity, with the possibility that a judge might come and rule contrary to that; so it is necessarily enforced. It is authentically reported that when Umar's preoccupations multiplied, he delegated the judiciary to Abu al-Darda', and two men brought their dispute to him. He ruled for one of them, then later reconsidered. The losing party asked Umar, who said: 'If I had been in his place, I would have ruled for you.' The man asked: 'What prevents you from judging?' He replied: 'There is no text here, and opinions are shared.' It is also narrated that Umar gave a ruling on an incident, then later gave a different ruling on it. When asked, he said: 'That was as we ruled, and this is as we rule now.' Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on when a judge must enforce a prior verdict. It defines the exceptions: contradiction to the well-known Sunnah (not rare reports) and consensus. The commentary explains that 'well-known' means widely transmitted, not ghareeb (isolated). A potential objection is raised: why not mention mutawatir? The answer is that the well-known is of lower grade than mutawatir, so if contradicting well-known prevents enforcement, then contradicting mutawatir does so a fortiori. Moreover, the Book and Sunnah referred to are those that are zanni al-dalalah (probable in meaning), because opposing qat'i al-dalalah would be kufr, not a mere judicial error. Consensus is defined as agreement that is not opposed by a valid legal argument. The reason for enforcement is that one ijtihad is not inherently superior to another; the first ruling is considered valid until overturned by definitive evidence, otherwise judicial decisions would be infinitely revocable. The historical precedent of Umar and Abu al-Darda' illustrates that judges may have different opinions, but the first ruling stands unless clearly wrong.

Key Terms
السنة المشهورة (al-sunnah al-mashhurah): The well-known Sunnah, meaning a hadith that is widely transmitted among the companions but not necessarily reaching mutawatir.
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids on a ruling, without any valid dissent based on a legal proof.
قطعي الدلالة (qat'i al-dalalah): A text that has definitive, unambiguous meaning; opposing it is kufr.
الغريب (al-ghareeb): A rare hadith, narrated by only one or few chains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the text specify 'well-known' (mashhurah) Sunnah rather than any authentic hadith?
A) To include mutawatir and exclude ahad.
B) To exclude rare (ghareeb) hadiths from overturning a judge's verdict.
C) Because only well-known hadiths are authentic.
D) To emphasize that only hadiths accepted by all schools are binding.

Key Takeaway
A prior judge's ruling must be enforced unless it contradicts the well-known Sunnah (not rare) or a consensus that has no valid opposing legal proof.
